The Meaningful Attribute: Two Interpretations
Defines the meaningful attribute with two opinions and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing definitions of the meaningful attribute (الصفة المعنوية) in classical kalam.

Core Idea
The 'meaningful attribute' can be defined either as caused or as non-necessary.

Now we turn to the second type: the meaningful attribute. The kalam scholars disagreed on its precise definition. Is it an attribute that is caused and additional to the essence, like our knowledge? Or is it simply a non-necessary attribute that may be absent? The commentator clarifies the implications of each view. Let's read the source passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two definitions are introduced by 'قال بعضهم' and 'وقيل'.
2
Understand the two definitions
The first definition calls it a 'caused attribute' (الصفة المعللة), meaning additional to the essence. The second calls it a 'non-necessary attribute' (الصفة الجائزة), meaning not necessarily predicated of its subject. Ask yourself how these differ.
The example 'like one of us' is key to understanding the first definition.
3
Examine the commentator's clarifications
The commentator explains why God's attributes are excluded from the first definition, and how the second definition overlaps with the fourth division of attributes. This shows the interconnectedness of the categories.
The phrase 'لا يخفى صدق هذا التفسير على القسم الرابع' is important for understanding the scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم (الثاني) الصفة المعنوية فقال بعضهم هي ( الصفة المعللة ) يعني زائداً على ذات الموصوف ككون احد منا عالماً قادراً (وقيل) الصفة المعنوية هي الصفة الجائزة ) أي : غير اللازمة الثبوت الموصوفها .
قوله : (منا إلخ ) بخلاف عالمية الواجب تعالى وقادريته فإنها غير معللة بمعنى زائد على الذات عندهم لنفيهم الصفات .
قوله : ( وقيل هي الصفة (الجائزة لا يخفى صدق هذا التفسير على القسم الرابع ولو على بعضه إلا أن يعتبر قيد آخر يخرجه أو يكتفي بالامتياز بالحيثيات .
The second division: the meaningful attribute. Some said it is the caused attribute (الصفة المعللة), meaning additional to the essence of the described, like one of us being knowing and powerful. And it was said: the meaningful attribute is the non-necessary attribute (الصفة الجائزة), i.e., non-necessary in its attribution to its described. His saying: 'like one of us, etc.' Contrary to the worldliness and powerfulness of the Necessary Exalted, for they are not caused by something additional to the essence according to them because of their denial of attributes. His saying: 'And it was said it is the non-necessary attribute' – it is not hidden that this interpretation is true of the fourth division, even if only of part of it, unless another condition is considered to exclude it or one is satisfied with distinction by considerations.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of attribute, the meaningful attribute (الصفة المعنوية). It presents two opinions. First, some define it as a caused attribute (الصفة المعللة), meaning an attribute that is additional to the essence of the described, exemplified by created beings being knowing and powerful. Second, it is defined as the non-necessary attribute (الصفة الجائزة), i.e., not necessary for the existence of its described. The commentator clarifies that the example 'like one of us' excludes the case of God's attributes, since according to the Mu'tazila who deny additional attributes, God's knowledge and power are not caused by something additional to the essence. The commentator also notes that the second interpretation (non-necessary attribute) also applies to the fourth division (attributes consequent upon origination), at least in part, unless an additional condition distinguishes them or one relies on different aspects.

Key Terms
الصفة المعنوية
الصفة المعللة
الصفة الجائزة
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The meaningful attribute always refers to the caused attribute (additional to the essence).
Correction: There is a second definition: the non-necessary attribute (الصفة الجائزة), which overlaps with the fourth division of attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the text, how does the second definition of the meaningful attribute (الصفة الجائزة) relate to the fourth division?
A) It applies completely to the fourth division
B) It applies only to the third division
C) It applies to the fourth division, at least partially
D) It does not apply at all

Key Takeaway
The meaningful attribute has two definitions in kalam: one emphasizes causality and additionalness to the essence; the other emphasizes contingency and non-necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge and power, do you tend to imagine them as qualities 'added' to His essence, even if unconsciously? This lesson reminds you that divine attributes are not caused or additional. How does that challenge your mental picture of God, and what will you do to correct it?
?
The second definition of meaningful attribute includes attributes that are not necessary—meaning they could be absent. But for God, every attribute is necessary. Have you ever treated God's mercy or justice as optional or conditional? How does this passage compel you to affirm that all of God's attributes are necessary and perfect?
?
This lesson draws a sharp line between human attributes (caused, additional) and divine attributes (uncaused, essential). Which areas of your life reflect a confusion between the two? Will you commit to studying the divine attributes more deeply so that your worship is based on true knowledge, not assumptions?

Why Origination is Neither Essential nor Meaningful
Defines origination and distinguishes it from essential and meaningful attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why origination is classified as an attribute resulting from the agent and why it is neither an essential nor a meaningful attribute.

Core Idea
Origination is an attribute that only appears with the agent's act.

Sanusi introduces a crucial distinction: attributes resulting from the agent are different from those inherent in the essence. He defines 'origination' as the paradigm case, and then carefully excludes it from two other categories of attributes: essential and meaningful. This passage sharpens our understanding of how kalam thinkers classify attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Focus on the definitions and the reasons given.
The passage includes a commentator's remark in parentheses.
2
Identify the three types of attributes mentioned
The passage distinguishes three types: essential, meaningful, and the attribute resulting from the agent (origination). Locate each and note the definitions.
3
Understand why origination is not essential or meaningful
Notice the two reasons: it is not essential because it is not present in non-existence; it is not meaningful because it is not caused by another attribute. Reflect on the significance of these distinctions.
This directly connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم ( الثالث) الصفة ( الحاصلة بالفاعل وهي) عندهم ( الحدوث وليست ( هذه الصفة أعني الحدوث صفة ( نفسية إذ لا تثبت حال العدم ) مع أن المعدوم الممكن عندهم متصف بكونه نفسياً . (ولا) صفة ( معوية لأنها لا تعلل بصفة )
قوله : (مع أن المعدوم إلخ ) لم يظهر لي فائدة هذه الضميمة مع أن الكلام تام بدونها لأنه إذا لم يكن ثابتاً حال العدم لم يكن عندهم صفة نفسية لأنها ثابتة حالتي الوجود والعدم .
قوله : ( ولا صفة معنوية لأنها لا تعلل إلخ ) هذا التعليل يدل على أنه أراد أن الحدوث ليس صفة معنوية بالاتفاق أي على التفسيرين وإلا فالظاهر أنه على التفسير الثاني منهما إذ الظاهر أن المراد بالصفة الجائزة غير اللازمة في حالتي الوجود والعدم والحدوث كذلك.
The third division: the attribute resulting from the agent, and it is, according to them, origination (الحدوث). And this attribute, I mean origination, is not an essential attribute because it is not established in the state of non-existence, although the possible non-existent is, according to them, described by being essential. Nor is it a meaningful attribute because it is not caused by an attribute. His saying: 'although the non-existent, etc.' I do not see the benefit of this addition, and the statement is complete without it, because if it is not established in the state of non-existence, then according to them it is not an essential attribute, because the essential attribute is established in both existence and non-existence. His saying: 'Nor is it a meaningful attribute because it is not caused, etc.' This proof indicates that he intended that origination is not a meaningful attribute by agreement, i.e., according to both interpretations; otherwise it is apparent that it is according to the second of them, because it is apparent that what is meant by the non-necessary attribute is that which is not necessary in the two states of existence and non-existence, and origination is likewise.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of attribute, the attribute resulting from the agent (الصفة الحاصلة بالفاعل), which is identified as origination (الحدوث). It then explains why origination is neither an essential attribute nor a meaningful attribute. It is not essential because it is not present in non-existence; the essential attribute must be shared by existent and non-existent. It is not meaningful because it is not caused by another attribute. The commentary questions the inclusion of the phrase 'although the possible non-existent is described by being essential,' noting it is unnecessary because the essential attribute's definition already requires existence in both states. The second commentary clarifies that the proof shows origination is not meaningful by either definition, especially the second definition of meaningful as non-necessary, because origination is not present in both states (it is absent in non-existence and present in existence).

Key Terms
الصفة الحاصلة بالفاعل
الحدوث
الصفة النفسية
الصفة المعنوية
المعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That origination could be considered an essential attribute because it is a necessary consequence of agency.
Correction: An essential attribute must be present in both existence and non-existence. Origination only exists when the agent acts, not in non-existence, so it fails the essential attribute condition.

Checkpoint
Why is origination (الحدوث) not considered a meaningful attribute according to the text?
A) Because it is not caused by a prior attribute
B) Because it is not established in non-existence
C) Because it is a necessary attribute
D) Because it is shared by existent and non-existent

Key Takeaway
Origination is a contingent attribute that appears only when an agent acts, and thus cannot be essential (permanent in both existence and non-existence) nor meaningful (not caused by another attribute).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. What attributes in your life—like health, wealth, or relationships—do you subconsciously treat as essential (permanent) when in fact they are originated and could vanish at any moment? If they were taken away, what would remain of your faith?
?
2. The lesson teaches that origination is not caused by an attribute but directly by the Agent. How often do you recognize that even your own actions are gifts from Allah, not stemming from an inherent power you possess? What would change if you lived with this awareness every moment?
?
3. If your entire identity is built on originated attributes (job, appearance, knowledge), and not on the essential nature of your soul's connection to Allah, what are you truly relying on? Will you commit today to detach your self-worth from these temporal things and anchor it in what is permanent in His sight?

The Attribute Consequent upon Origination: Necessary and Possible
Defines attributes consequent upon origination and lists their necessary and possible subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the attribute consequent upon origination, how it differs from other attributes, and its two subdivisions: necessary and possible.

Core Idea
Attributes consequent upon origination are not inherent in non-existence and not caused by the agent.

This passage introduces the fourth category of attributes in the Islamic theological scheme. These attributes only appear when something comes into existence, and they are not directly produced by the agent. Understanding this distinction is crucial for later discussions on divine attributes and causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the definition and the two subdivisions with examples.
The passage begins by defining the attribute and then enumerates necessary and possible types.
2
Distinguish from other attributes
Pay attention to the commentary that distinguishes this attribute from the essential attribute and from existence itself. This clarifies the scope of the definition.
The phrase 'no realization in non-existence' sets it apart from essential attributes.
3
Identify necessary vs. possible
List the examples given for necessary attributes (spatial occupation, acceptance of accidents, etc.) and for possible attributes (actions being obedience or disobedience). Notice that possible attributes depend on will.
The commentary modifies some examples, such as inherence during annihilation.
4
Reflect on the commentary
Understand the precautionary clauses: 'the agent has no effect' means primarily, and the limitation on inherence and contrariety for accidents.
This prevents misunderstanding that these attributes are directly caused by the agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم ( الرابع ) الصفة ( التابعة للحدوث وهي التي لا تحقق لها في حالة العدم ولا يتصف بها الممكن إلا بعد وجوده ) ولا تأثير للفاعل فيها ) وهي منقسمة إلى أقسام (فمنها ما هي ( واجبة ) أي : يجب حصولها لموصوفها عند حدوثه. ) كالتحيز وقبول الأعراض للجوهر وكالحلول في المحال والتضاد للأعراض وكإيجاب العلة معلولها وقبح القبيح فإن هذه كلها صفات واجبة الحصول لموصوفاتها عند حدوثها . ( ومنها ما هي ( ممكنة ) أي : غير واجبة الحصول لموصوفها عند حدوثه ) وهي إما ( تابعة للإرادة ككون الفعل ) الصادر من العبد . ( طاعة أو معصية) وتعظيماً
قوله : ( وهي التي لا تحقق إلخ ) بهذا يمتاز عن الصفة النفسية والحدوث.
قوله : ( ولا يتصف إلخ ) احتراز عن الوجود .
قوله : ( ولا تأثير إلخ) أي: أصالة .
قوله : ( وكالحلول في المحال والتضاد للأعراض لا يصح إلا بالنسبة إلى بعض الأعراض لعدم الحلول في الفناء على ما سيأتي إلا عند بعض المعتزلة .
The fourth division: the attribute consequent upon origination, and it is that which has no realization in the state of non-existence, and the possible thing is not described by it except after its existence, and the agent has no effect on it. And it is divided into sections: among them are those which are necessary, i.e., their occurrence for their described at the time of its origination is necessary, such as spatial occupation and acceptance of accidents for the substance, and such as inherence in place and contrariety for accidents, and such as the cause necessitating its effect, and the ugliness of the ugly; for all these are attributes whose occurrence for their described at their origination is necessary. And among them are those which are possible, i.e., not necessary in their occurrence for their described at its origination, and they are either consequent upon will, like the action proceeding from the servant being obedience or disobedience, and glorification. His saying: 'And it is that which has no realization, etc.' By this it is distinguished from the essential attribute and origination. His saying: 'And the possible thing is not described, etc.' A precaution to exclude existence. His saying: 'And the agent has no effect, etc.' i.e., primarily. His saying: 'And such as inherence in place and contrariety for accidents' – this is not valid except with respect to some accidents, because there is no inherence in annihilation, as will come, except according to some Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of attribute, the attribute consequent upon origination (الصفة التابعة للحدوث). It is characterized by having no realization in non-existence; the possible thing only acquires it after existence, and the agent has no effect on it (i.e., it is not directly brought about by the agent). It is subdivided into two categories: necessary attributes, which inevitably occur for their subject at the time of origination, such as spatial occupation (التحيز) for a body, acceptance of accidents for a substance, inherence in a place for accidents, contrariety for accidents, causal necessitation, and the ugliness of evil. And possible attributes, which are not necessary, and they are further divided into those dependent on will, such as an action being obedience or disobedience, and glorification (or 'and respect'). The commentary explains that this definition distinguishes this type from the essential attribute (which is present in non-existence) and from origination (which is present only at the moment of origination, not after). It also clarifies that the phrase 'the possible thing is not described by it except after its existence' excludes existence itself. The comment on 'no effect of the agent' means the agent has no primary effect on these attributes. The final comment modifies the example of inherence and contrariety, noting that inherence does not apply during annihilation (فناء) except according to some Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
الصفة التابعة للحدوث
واجبة
ممكنة
التحيز
قبول الأعراض
الحلول
التضاد
إيجاب العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That these attributes are caused by the agent acting directly.
Correction: The text explicitly states 'ولا تأثير للفاعل فيها' – the agent has no effect on them.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a necessary attribute consequent upon origination according to the text?
A) Obedience or disobedience of an action
B) Spatial occupation for a substance
C) Knowledge for a human
D) Color for a body

Key Takeaway
The attribute consequent upon origination is defined by its non-existence before origination and independence from the agent's causation; it is either necessary or possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You exist now, occupying space and having a body. This is a necessary attribute consequent upon your origination—you did not choose it, and no agent caused it directly. Does this make you feel more dependent on the One who brought you into existence, or less? How will you respond to that dependence today?
?
Among the possible attributes, your actions can be obedience or disobedience. You choose which they become. Think of a recent action: was it obedience or disobedience? Knowing that this attribute is up to you, what concrete step will you take tomorrow to make one of your actions an act of obedience?
?
The text lists 'ugliness of the ugly' as a necessary attribute. When you see something you know is ugly—a sin, an injustice—do you simply accept it as necessary, or does it move you to action? What will you do the next time you encounter something ugly that you can change?

Possible Attributes: Will-Dependent and Knowledge-Dependent
Discusses examples and classification of possible attributes, with disagreements on will and good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between possible attributes dependent on will and those dependent on knowledge, and the theological disagreements about the scope of will and the nature of good and evil.

Core Idea
Intentions matter: what makes an act good or evil depends on whether it was willed and known.

In this passage, the author continues discussing possible attributes by examining how intention and knowledge qualify an action. He shows that the same act can be blameworthy or praiseworthy based on the agent's intent, and that the good (al-husn) and the ugly (al-qubh) are subject to debate: are they necessary properties or contingent on will? These distinctions are foundational for understanding the Ash'ari position on moral evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get a clear sense of the author's argument.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Examine the examples
Notice how an action becomes an act of humiliation or a command only with intention. Reflect on how intention qualifies an action.
3
Distinguish the two categories
Identify the two categories of possible attributes: those dependent on will and those dependent on knowledge. Note the condition for the latter: the agent must know the object.
4
Understand the agreements and disagreements
Note that knowledge-dependent attributes are agreed upon (no difference between necessary and acquired knowledge), but will-dependent attributes have a disagreement: some say only created will is effective, others include necessary will.
5
Analyze the disagreement on al-husn
Finally, examine the dispute concerning 'the good' (al-husn): whether it is a necessary attribute (like al-qubh) or conditional upon will. This touches on theological debates about morality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو إهانة فإن الفعل قد يوجد غير متصف بشيء من ذلك إذا لم يكن هناك قصد وإرادة وككون الأمر أمراً فإنَّ قول القائل : أفعل قد يوجد ولا يكون أمراً إذا لم يكن قصد إلى طلب الفعل ( و ) إما ( غيرها ) أي غير تابعة هو الإرادة بشرط كون الفاعل عالما به وقد اتفقوا على أنَّ ما يؤثر فيه العلم لا فرق فيه بين العلم الضروري وغير الضروري لكن اختلفوا فيما يؤثر فيه الإرادة فقال بعضهم : المؤثر من الإرادات ما كان مقدوراً مخترعاً للمريد دون ما كان منها ضرورياً وقال الآخرون : لا فرق بين الإرادتين كما لا فرق بين العلمين (و) بينهم خلاف (في الحسن أهو مما يتبع الحدوث وجوباً) كالقبيح فيكون من قبيل الواجبة ( أو ) هو مما يتبع الحدوث مشروطاً ( بالإرادة ) فيكون من قبيل الممكنة التابعة للإرادة . قوله : ( وككون الأمر أمراً ) أي كون الصيغة المخصوصة طلباً للفعل استعلاء. قوله : ( تابعة لحدوث العلم ولذا لا يتصف علم الباري بشيء من الضرورة والكس قوله : ( ما كان مقدوراً إلخ ) وإرادتنا مقدورة مخترعة عندهم بناء على أنهم فسروا الإرادة لا الصفة المرجحة فلا يرد أنه لو كانت الإرادة مقدورة لزم تسلسل بميل يتبع اعتقاد النفع الإرادات . قوله : ( بلا قصد وإرادة قيل عدم القصد ممنوع غايته عدم الشعور به . ) قوله : ( بشرط كون الفاعل عالماً (به وإلا فمجرد إرادة الفاعل أتقان فعله لا يؤثر فيه . قوله : (ما كان مقدوراً مخترعاً للمريد) فإن قلت : إرادتنا ليست مقدورة لنا أصلاً وإلا احتاج حصوله فينا إلى إرادة أخرى وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى قلت : هذا إنما يلزم إذا فسرت بالصفة المخصصة لأحد طرفي المقدور بالوقوع كما هو مذهب أهل السنة وأما إذا فسرت بالميل التابع للاعتقاد بالنفع أو بنفس ذلك الاعتقاد فيجوز أن تكون مقدورة ومخترعة كما سيجيء في بحث الإرادة .
Or humiliation, for the action may occur unqualified by any of that if there is no intention and will. And like the command being a command, for the speaker's saying 'Do!' may occur and not be a command if there is no intention to demand the action. And either other than [dependent on will], i.e., not dependent on it, [but] on the condition that the agent is knowing of it. And they have agreed that that which knowledge affects, there is no difference therein between necessary knowledge and non-necessary; but they differed concerning that which will affects. Some said: the effective among wills is that which is potential and invented by the willer, not that which is necessary. And others said: there is no difference between the two wills just as there is no difference between the two knowledges. And among them there is disagreement concerning the good (الحسن): whether it is among that which follows origination necessarily like the ugly (القبيح), so it belongs to the category of necessary, or whether it is among that which follows origination conditionally upon will, so it belongs to the category of possible dependent on will. His statement: 'and like the command being a command' i.e., that the specific form is a demand for the action in a superior manner. His statement: 'dependent on the origination of knowledge, and therefore the knowledge of the All-Powerful is not qualified by anything of the necessary and ...' His statement: 'what was potential etc.' and our will is potential and invented according to them, based on their interpretation that will is not the preferential attribute, so it is not objected that if will were potential, an infinite regress of wills would follow, [since it is] a inclination following belief in benefit. His statement: 'without intention and will' it was said that the absence of intention is rejected; at most it is lack of awareness of it. His statement: 'on condition that the agent is knowing of it; otherwise, mere will of the agent for the perfection of his action does not affect it.' His statement: 'what was potential and invented for the willer' for if you say: our will is not potential for us at all, otherwise its occurrence in us would require another will, and so on ad infinitum, I say: this is necessary only if it is interpreted as the attribute that specifies one of the two sides of the potential to occur, as is the doctrine of the Sunnis; but if it is interpreted as the inclination following belief in benefit, or that belief itself, then it is permissible that it be potential and invented, as will come in the discussion of will.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage completes the discussion of possible attributes (ممكنة) by adding more examples and then moving to attributes dependent on knowledge (غير تابعة للإرادة). It begins with the example of an action being an act of humiliation (إهانة) if intended, showing that the same action can be qualified differently depending on intention. Similarly, a command (الأمر) is not a command without the intention to demand. Then it introduces another category of possible attributes: those that are not dependent on will (غير تابعة للإرادة) but on knowledge, requiring that the agent be aware of the object. There is agreement that knowledge-based attributes apply equally whether the knowledge is necessary or acquired. However, there is disagreement regarding will-based attributes: some say only will that is potential and invented (مقدور مخترع) is effective, while others say even necessary will (الضرورية) is effective. Then a disagreement about the good (الحسن) is mentioned: whether it necessarily follows origination like the ugly (القبيح), making it a necessary attribute, or whether it is conditional upon will, making it a possible attribute dependent on will. The commentary clarifies: 'like the command being a command' specifies the linguistic form used as a command. 'Dependent on the origination of knowledge' explains why God's knowledge is not qualified by being necessary or acquired. The objection about infinite regress if will is potential is answered by noting that will is interpreted as an inclination following belief in benefit, not as a selective attribute, so it can be created without regress. The commentator also addresses the point that 'without intention and will' does not mean absence of intention but rather lack of awareness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Good and evil are always intrinsic properties of actions independent of divine command.
Correction: The passage shows a disagreement: some say they follow origination necessarily, others say conditionally upon will, reflecting the Ash'ari position that moral values are determined by divine will.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on what point do scholars disagree regarding will-based attributes?
A) Whether necessary knowledge differs from acquired knowledge
B) Whether only potential and invented will is effective or also necessary will
C) Whether the good is dependent on knowledge
D) Whether actions can be without intention

Key Takeaway
The same action can be qualified differently based on intention, and while knowledge-based attributes are uniform, will-based attributes and moral values like good and evil are subject to theological disagreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you ever do it without truly intending it? How does that affect the moral reality of that action in your own sight and before Allah? This lesson shows that without intention, an act may not even be considered a command or humiliation. What actions of yours might be empty of true intention, and what would it cost you to make them intentional?
?
The scholars disagreed on whether good (الحسن) is necessary or dependent on will. This debate is not merely academic; it touches on why Allah commands what He does. Do you live as though good is good because Allah wills it, or as though Allah wills it because it is good? Which view makes you more submissive to His commands, and which makes you more likely to question them?
?
Your own will is something you experience every day, but this passage reveals it can be defined in ways that avoid contradiction. Have you ever considered that your inclinations and beliefs about benefit shape your will more than you realize? What beliefs about benefit are driving your current choices, and are they aligned with the truth that Allah is the ultimate benefit?

The Ash'ari Definition of Accident
Ash'ari definition and its defense against alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Ash'ari definition of accident as 'an existent subsisting in a spatial entity' is carefully chosen to exclude non-existents, negations, substances, and the divine essence and attributes, and also why an alternative definition is rejected.

Core Idea
Accidents are real, dependent existents that require a spatial substance.

The Ash'ari school defines accident with precision to avoid confusion with other categories. This definition serves as a foundational concept in kalam, enabling theologians to discuss the properties of contingent beings. In this lesson, you will examine the definition itself, its rationale, and why alternative proposals fail.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari definition
Read the Arabic text of the definition and its translation. Note the key terms: 'mawjud', 'qa'im bi mutahayyiz'.
The definition is short but dense.
2
Understand the exclusions
The text lists what the definition excludes: non-existents, negations, substances, and the divine essence and attributes. Ask yourself why each is excluded.
This shows the definition's precision.
3
Consider the alternative definition and its refutation
Some Ash'aris defined accident as 'an attribute of something else'. See why this fails: it would include negative attributes and, if attributes are distinct, divine attributes. This refinement clarifies the limits of the term 'accident'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول في أبحاثه الكلية الشاملة لجميع الأعراض ( وفيه مقاصد ) . المقصد الأول : تعريف العرض ] هو هذا ( في تعريف العرض (أما ) تعريفه : ( عندنا فموجود قائم بمتحيز) المختار في تعريفه لأنه خرج منه الأعدام والسلوب إذ ليست موجودة والجواهر إذ هي غير قائمة بمتحيز وخرج أيضاً ذات الرب وصفاته ومعنى القيام بالغير هو الناعت أو التبعية في التحيز والأول هو الصحيح كما ستعرفه وقال : بعض الأشاعرة العرض ما كان صفة لغيره وهو منقوض بالصفات السلبية فإنها صفة لغيرها وليست أعراضاً لأن العَرَض من أقسام الموجود ومنقوض أيضاً بصفاته تعالى إذا قيل بالتغاير بين الذات والصفات ( وأما ) تعريفه ( عند المعتزلة فما لو وجد لقام بالمتحيز ) وإنما اختاروا هذا التعريف ( لأنَّه ) أي العرض ( ثابت في العدم عندهم ) منفك عن الوجود الذي هو قوله : ( كما ستعرفه) في بحث امتناع قيام العرض بالعرض. قوله : ( وهو منقوض إلخ إلا أن يخص كلمة ما بالموجود . قوله : (بصفاته إلخ) فإنها ليست بأعراض بناء على أن العرض قشم التعريف عليها إذا قيل بالغيرية بين الذات والصفات وإلا فخارجة بقيد الغيرية. الحادث صدق مع قوله : ( ولا يقوم إلخ ) بناء على قولهم بأن الثابت في العدم ذات المعدومات من غير قيام بعضها ببعض فإنه من خواص الوجود إلا عند بعضهم فإنهم قالوا باتصاف المعدومات الثابتة بالصفات المعدومة الثابتة وقد ذلك . مر قوله: (وهو منقوض بالصفات السلبية) وبالأعدام أيضاً فإما أن يحال على المقايسة أو يفسر الصفات السلبية بما يتناول الأعدام . قوله : إذا قيل بالتغاير بين الذات والصفات) وأما إذا لم يقل بذلك فيخرج بقيد الغير وهذا إنما يصح إذا خص عدم التغاير بالصفات القديمة كما صرح به البعض وإلا يخرج جميع الأعراض لأنها ليست غير الذات عند البعض كما سبق تفصيله. قوله فما لو وجد لقام بالمتحيز) قيل : الأولى أن يقال : فما إذا وجد ليفهم إمكان الوجود فيخرج الأعدام والسلوب ولك أن تمنع كون السلوب والأعدام على تقدير وجودها قائمة بالمتحيز لجواز قيامها بنفسها بناء على أن وجودها محال جاز أن يستلزم محالاً آخر. نعم لو بدل لفظ لو بإذا لظهر خروجها عن التعريف وشمولها للموجود بالفعل.
The first observatory (al-marsad al-awwal) on its universal investigations encompassing all accidents (and it contains objectives). The first objective: definition of accident (al-'arad). This is it. (Regarding the definition of accident:) As for its definition according to us (Ash'aris), it is an existent subsisting in a spatial entity (mutahayyiz). The chosen (definition) because it excludes non-existents and negations (since they are not existent), and substances (since they do not subsist in a spatial entity), and also excludes the essence of the Lord and His attributes. And the meaning of 'subsistence in another' (al-qiyam bi al-ghayr) is being an attribute or following in spatiality, and the first is correct as you will know. And some Ash'aris said: accident is that which is an attribute of something else, and it is refuted by negative attributes, for they are attributes of something else but are not accidents, because accident is one of the divisions of existence. And it is also refuted by His attributes (may He be exalted) if one says there is differentiation between essence and attributes. As for its definition according to the Mu'tazila: it is that which, if it existed, would subsist in a spatial entity. And they chose this definition because accident, according to them, is established in non-existence, separate from existence which is... His saying: (as you will know) in the discussion of the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident. His saying: (and it is refuted etc.) except that the word 'what' is specified to the existent. His saying: (by His attributes etc.) for they are not accidents, based on the fact that accident is a division... the definition applies to them if there is differentiation between essence and attributes, otherwise they are excluded by the restriction of otherness. The occurrence is true with His saying: (and it does not subsist etc.) based on their statement that what is established in non-existence is the essence of non-existents without some subsisting in others, for it is a property of existence except according to some of them, for they said that non-existents that are established are characterized by non-existent attributes that are established, and that has passed. He said: (and it is refuted by negative attributes) and by non-existents as well, so it is either referred to analogy or negative attributes are interpreted to include non-existents. His saying: if one says there is differentiation between essence and attributes) and if one does not say that, then it is excluded by the restriction of otherness, and this is only correct if non-differentiation is restricted to eternal attributes as some have explained, otherwise all accidents would be excluded because they are not other than the essence according to some, as previously detailed. His saying: (so that if it existed, it would subsist in a spatial entity) it is said: it would be better to say: so if it existed, to understand possibility of existence, so it excludes non-existents and negations. And you may deny that negations and non-existents, supposing their existence, would subsist in a spatial entity, because their existence is impossible, it is possible that it entails another impossibility. Yes, if the word 'law' (if) is replaced by 'idha' (when), its exclusion from the definition and its inclusion of actually existent things would become apparent.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'accident' (al-'arad) within the kalam framework, presenting the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila definitions and addressing criticisms. It begins by stating the first objective of the first observatory: the definition of accident. The Ash'ari definition is 'an existent subsisting in a spatial entity' (mawjud qa'im bi mutahayyiz). The author explains why this definition is chosen: it excludes non-existents and negations (which are not existent), substances (which do not subsist in a spatial entity), and the divine essence and attributes. He then clarifies that 'subsistence in another' means being an attribute or following in spatiality, with the first interpretation being correct. An alternative Ash'ari definition—'accident is that which is an attribute of something else'—is considered but refuted on two grounds: negative attributes (e.g., blindness) are attributes of something else but are not accidents (since accidents are existent), and divine attributes (if distinct from essence) would also be excluded if they were accidents, but they are not. The Mu'tazila definition is 'that which, if it existed, would subsist in a spatial entity' (ma law wajada laqama bi mutahayyiz). They adopt this because they hold that accidents are established in non-existence, separate from existence. The commentary then addresses objections: the word 'what' (ma) in the Ash'ari definition should be restricted to existent things; divine attributes are not accidents if they are identical with the essence; the Mu'tazila definition is refined by replacing 'law' (if) with 'idha' (when) to clearly exclude non-existents and include actual existents. The passage also touches on the issue of non-existents being characterized by attributes according to some Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
العرض
القائم بمتحيز
الجوهر
الناعت
التبعية في التحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any attribute of something else is an accident.
Correction: Accidents are a subset of attributes: they must be existent qualities. Negative attributes like blindness are not accidents because they are not existent, and divine attributes are not accidents because they are not dependent on a spatial entity.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT excluded by the Ash'ari definition of accident as 'an existent subsisting in a spatial entity'?
A) Non-existents
B) Substances
C) Accidents themselves
D) The essence of Allah

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari definition of accident as an existent subsisting in a spatial entity is carefully crafted to distinguish accidents from non-existents, negations, substances, and the divine, and it avoids the pitfalls of a broader definition based solely on being an attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe yourself with negative traits like 'weak' or 'lazy', are you treating these negations as real accidents? How does knowing they are non-existents change the weight you give them, and will you now actively replace them with true descriptions rooted in your actual existent qualities?
?
The definition of accident excludes Allah's attributes. Does this challenge how you think about your own relationship with God? If His attributes are not like accidents, how should that shape your worship and reliance on Him?
?
The Ash'ari definition required precision to avoid error. What area of your theology might be imprecise enough to accidentally include false beliefs? Will you commit to studying definitions like this to guard your creed?

The Mu'tazila Definition of Accident
Mu'tazila definition of accident and a refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila definition of accident as 'that which, if it existed, would subsist in a spatial entity', and why they used a conditional phrase, as well as the refinement of replacing 'law' with 'idha'.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, accidents are defined by their potential dependence on substances, even if they are considered in non-existence.

The Mu'tazila define accident conditionally because they hold that accidents are established in non-existence. This definition differs from the Ash'ari one in that it does not require actual existence. In this lesson, you will examine the definition, its rationale, and a proposed refinement to avoid including non-existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila definition
Read the Arabic: 'fama law wajada laqama bi mutahayyiz'. Compare it to the Ash'ari definition.
Notice the conditional 'law'.
2
Understand why they use this definition
The Mu'tazila believe accidents are 'thabit fi al-'adam' (established in non-existence). So they define accident not as actually existing, but as something that, if it were to exist, would subsist in a spatial entity.
3
Consider the refinement
The author mentions that replacing 'law' with 'idha' would better exclude non-existents and include actual existents. Think about why 'idha' (when) indicates actual existence or possibility.

?

?

Accidents Specific to Animals: Classification and Refutation
Classification of accidents and refutation of a specific ten-fold enumeration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how accidents are classified (especially those specific to animals) and why the claim that these accidents are exactly ten is false.

Core Idea
The ten-list of animal accidents is obviously incomplete; many like laughter are excluded.

This passage from al-Sharh al-Mawaqif presents the theological classification of accidents, focusing on those that are proper to living beings (animals). The author then targets an enumeration that limits these accidents to ten, showing its falsity by pointing out missing attributes. Let's read the text and its commentary to grasp this important point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on accident classification
Start by reading the passage that defines accidents specific to animals (life, perceptions, etc.) and those not specific. Notice the distinction.
The passage uses a binary division: accidents specific to the living and others.
2
Study the commentary on perceptions
The commentator explains why sense perception is not considered knowledge. Pay attention to the nuance.
This clarifies the term 'perceptions by the senses'.
3
Read the refutation of the ten enumeration
The text states that enumerating these accidents as ten is false. Read the list from al-Saha'if and the commentator's criticism.
The list includes life, power, belief, etc.
4
Reflect on the missing attributes
The commentator lists missing attributes like wonder, laughter, joy, grief. Consider why the list is incomplete.
This shows the enumeration is not exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثاني : العرض وأقسامه عند المتكلمين ]
( في أقسامه عند المتكلمين (وهو) أي العرض (إما أن يختص بالحي وهو الحياة وما يتبعها من الإدراكات بالحواس (و) من ( غيرها كالعلم والقدرة ) والإرادة
كالمفارقات والهيولى والجسم أو يكون في محل لكن لا يكون مقوماً له كالصورة بالقياس إلى الهيولى .

قوله : ( كالعلم إلخ) مثال لغيرها بناء على أنَّ الإدراك الحسي ليس من العلم ولذا زاد بعضهم قيد بين المعاني في تعريفه كما مر .

قوله : ( من الإدراكات بالحواس) لم يجعل قوله كالعلم مثالاً للإدراكات على طريق اللف والنشر لأنَّ المشهور استعمال الإحساس في الإدراك ولأنَّ الأنسب حينئذ كالعلوم.

وحصرها في عشرة باطل بلا شبهة

قوله : ( وحصرها إلخ) كما حصرها صاحب الصحائف في عشرة الحياة والقدرة والاعتقاد والظن وكلام النفس والإرادة والكراهة والشهوة والنفرة والألم كذا نقله بعض الناظرين وبطلانه أظهر من أن يخفى على من له أدنى فطانة ولعمري كيف خفي على ذلك الفاضل

قوله : ( وحصرها في عشرة باطل حصرها صاحب الصحائف في الحياة والقدرة والاعتقاد والظن وكلام النفس والإرادة والكراهة والشهوة والنفرة والألم ولا يخفى بطلانه لخروج التعجب والضحك والفرح والغم وأمثالها .
[Second Goal: The accident and its divisions according to the theologians] (In its divisions according to the theologians, and it, i.e., the accident, is either specific to the animal, which is life and what follows from it of perceptions by the senses, and from other than them like knowledge and power, and will) like the separate intellects, matter, and body, or is in a locus but is not constitutive of it like form in relation to matter. His saying: ('like knowledge etc.') is an example of other than them based on the fact that sense perception is not knowledge, so some added a restriction 'among meanings' in its definition as passed. His saying: ('from perceptions by the senses') he did not make his saying 'like knowledge' an example of perceptions by way of lard and nashr [literary devices] because the well-known is the use of sensation in perception, and because then the more appropriate would be 'like knowledges'. And their enumeration as ten is false without doubt. His saying: ('and their enumeration etc.') as the author of al-Saha'if enumerated them as ten: life, power, belief, opinion, speech of the soul, will, aversion, desire, disgust, and pain, as some investigators have transmitted; its falsehood is clearer than to be hidden from anyone with the least intelligence, and indeed how could it be hidden from that virtuous person? His saying: ('and their enumeration as ten is false') the author of al-Saha'if enumerated them as life, power, belief, opinion, speech of the soul, will, aversion, desire, disgust, and pain, and its falsehood is not hidden because of the exclusion of wonder, laughter, joy, grief, and the like.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage classifies accidents and focuses on those specific to animals. The author first states the division: an accident is either specific to the animal (i.e., life and its accompanying perceptions and other attributes like knowledge, power, and will) or not. He gives examples of non-specific accidents (like separate intellects, matter, body, or form in a subject). Then he addresses the enumeration of these specific accidents. The commentator explains that 'like knowledge' is an example of non-sensory perceptions because sense perception is distinct from knowledge. He also notes that the phrase 'perceptions by the senses' shows that 'like knowledge' is not meant as an example of sensory perceptions. The matn then declares that the enumeration of these specific accidents as exactly ten is false. The commentator clarifies that the author of al-Saha'if listed them as ten: life, power, belief, opinion, speech of the soul, will, aversion, desire, disgust, and pain. The commentator strongly rejects this enumeration, arguing that it obviously leaves out many like wonder, laughter, joy, grief, etc. The falsehood is claimed to be apparent to anyone with basic intelligence.

Correction: The passage shows this is false because many other accidents (e.g., wonder, laughter, joy, grief) are not included in the list.

Non-Specific Accidents: Al-Akwan and Sensible Qualities
Definition of al-akwan and sensible qualities as non-specific accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of accidents not specific to animals: al-akwan (the four types) and sensible qualities, as defined in classical kalam.

Core Idea
Al-akwan are the four universal accidents: motion, rest, combination, and separation.

In this passage, Sanusi begins by distinguishing accidents that are specific to animals from those that are not. He then introduces the two main categories of non-specific accidents: the four al-akwan (motion, rest, combination, separation) and the qualities perceived by the five senses. This classification forms the foundation for understanding accidental properties in the created world.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get an overview of the two categories. Note that al-akwan are defined by four types, and sensible qualities are listed with examples.
The passage also mentions a later discussion about the confinement of akwan to these four.
2
Identify the two categories
Find where the text defines 'non-specific accidents' and lists the two categories: al-akwan and sensible qualities. Notice that al-akwan are further divided into motion, rest, combination, and separation.
3
Reflect on the classification
Consider why these accidents are called 'non-specific'. They are universal—they can apply to animals, plants, minerals, etc. Sensible qualities are those perceived by the five senses.
This classification is crucial for later discussions on causality and perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وإما أن لا يختص به وهو الأكوان ( المنحصرة في أنواع أربعة : الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق، ( والمحسوسات ) بإحدى الحواس الخمس كالأصوات والألوان والروائح والطعوم والحرارة وأخواتها وذهب بعضهم إلى أن الأكوان محسوسة بالضرورة ومن أنكر الأكوان فقد كابر حسه ومقتضى عقله، وآخرون إلى أنها غير محسوسة فإنَّا لا نشاهد إلا المتحرك الساكن والمجتمعين والمفترقين وأما وصف الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق فلا ولهذا اختلف في كونها وجودية ولو كانت محسوسة لما وقع الخلاف فيها

قوله: (محسوسة بالضرورة) أي بالبصر فهي داخلة في المحسوسات ومنشأ هذا القول عدم الفرق بين المحسوس بالذات وبين المحسوس بالواسطة .

قوله : ( لما وقع الخلاف إذ لا شبهة في وجود المحسوسات وإن كابره منكرو الحسيات.

قوله : (المنحصرة في أنواع (أربعة سيأتي في بحث الأكوان المناقشة في الحصر بالكون الأول وجوابها على التفصيل .

قوله : ( لما وقع الخلاف فيها أي بين كثيرين، وإلا فسيجيء أن بعضاً من القدماء قال : لا وجود للألوان مع أنها محسوسة.
(And either it is not specific to it, i.e., al-akwan, confined to four types: motion, rest, combination, and separation; and sensible things by one of the five senses, like sounds, colors, smells, tastes, heat and its sisters. And some went to that al-akwan are necessarily sensible, and whoever denies al-akwan has disbelieved his sense and the requirement of his intellect; and others to that they are not sensible, for we only witness the moving, the stationary, the combined, and the separated; as for the description of motion, rest, combination, and separation, no; and therefore they differed on whether they are existent, and if they were sensible there would not have been disagreement about them. His saying: ('necessarily sensible') i.e., by sight, so they are included among sensible things, and the origin of this opinion is the lack of distinction between the intrinsically sensible and the mediately sensible. His saying: ('there would not have been disagreement') because there is no doubt about the existence of sensible things even if deniers of the sensibles fight them. His saying: ('confined to four types') there will come in the discussion of al-akwan the discussion about the confinement to the first kind and its answer in detail. His saying: ('there would not have been disagreement about them') i.e., among many; otherwise, it will come that some of the ancients said there is no existence for colors even though they are sensible.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage defines the accidents that are not specific to animals, namely al-akwan (motion, rest, combination, separation) and sensible qualities. It then presents a dispute about whether al-akwan are sensible. One group holds they are necessarily sensible, denying them would contradict sense and reason. Another group holds they are not sensible because we only perceive the objects that are moving or at rest, not the accidents themselves. The commentator explains that the first view arises from confusing the intrinsically sensible (the accident itself) with the mediately sensible (the object that has the accident). He adds that if akwan were sensible, there would be no disagreement about their existence, but their existence is disputed. The commentator also notes that the confinement of akwan to four types will be discussed later, and that some ancients even denied the existence of colors despite their being sensible.

A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Non-specific accidents consist of two categories: the four akwan (motion, rest, combination, separation) and sensible qualities perceived by the five senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience motion and rest in your daily life, do you ever pause to consider that these are not permanent but accidents that point to a Sustainer who holds everything in existence? How can you remind yourself of this dependency throughout your day?
?
Think about a sound, color, or smell you encountered today. That quality is a temporary accident, not the essence of the thing. How does knowing this change the way you value sensory pleasures? What step can you take today to detach your heart from them?
?
The four akwan—motion, rest, combination, separation—are all around you. Can you identify one example of each in your immediate environment right now? As you name them, ask yourself: Who is the ultimate cause of these changes? Let that question lead you to a sincere moment of gratitude or reflection.

The Dispute Over the Sensibility of Al-Akwan
Dispute on whether al-akwan are necessarily sensible or not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing views on whether al-akwan are sensible and the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
Al-akwan are not directly sensible; we only perceive their bearers.

After defining al-akwan, Sanusi presents a dispute: are these accidents themselves perceptible by the senses? Some scholars hold that motion, rest, combination, and separation are necessarily sensible, while others argue they are only known indirectly through the objects they modify. This disagreement has implications for the ontological status of accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage on the dispute
Read the Arabic and translation from 'وذهب بعضهم' to the end. Identify the two groups and their arguments.
Note the commentator's explanation of the first group's error.
2
Analyze the arguments
First group: akwan are sensible by necessity. Second group: we only see moving objects, not motion itself. Which side does the author seem to favor?
The commentator points out that if akwan were sensible, there would be no disagreement about their existence.
3
Reflect on the philosophical point
Understand the distinction between 'intrinsically sensible' and 'mediately sensible'. Al-akwan are not directly perceived but inferred from changes in bodies.
This dispute touches on the nature of perception and existence of accidents.

Finiteness of the Types of Accidents in Existence
Proving finiteness of accidental types via induction and proof of application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that the types of accidents existing in the world are finite, including the use of induction and the proof of application.

Core Idea
The number of accidental types is finite, shown by induction and logical application.

This passage addresses a key question in Islamic theology: are the types of accidents (properties inhering in substances) finite? The author asserts that they are, and provides two proofs: empirical induction and a logical argument known as the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq). The commentator clarifies that the finiteness of each division implies the finiteness of the total, a subtle point that reinforces the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words and the commentator's clarification come through before interpreting.
The passage has two parts: the author's statement and the commentator's note.
2
Identify the two proofs
The author cites two pieces of evidence: induction (empirical observation) and the proof of application (a logical argument). Find each in the text and note how they are presented.
The proof of application is a well-known kalam argument for finiteness.
3
Understand the commentator's clarification
The commentator explains that the finiteness of each division's types does not directly state the total is finite, but it follows because the divisions themselves are finite. Make sure you see the logical step.
This prevents a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن أنواع كل واحد من هذه الأقسام المندرجة تحت المختصة بالحي وغير المختصة به ( متناهية بحسب الوجود) يعني أن عدد الأنواع العرضية الموجودة متناه ) دل عليه الاستقراء ) وبرهان التطبيق أيضا

قوله : ( يعني أن عدد إلخ) أفاد بالعناية إلى أنَّ المستفاد من المتن وإن كانت تناهي أنواع كل واحد من هذه الأقسام لا مجموعها لكنه يلزم ذلك بناء على تناهي تلك الأقسام .
And know that the types of each one of these divisions falling under the specific to the animal and the non-specific are finite according to existence, meaning the number of existing accidental types is finite; induction indicates it, and also the proof of application. His saying: ('meaning the number etc.') he pointed out that what is understood from the text is that the finiteness of the types of each of these divisions is not of the total, but it follows from the finiteness of those divisions.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the types of accidents existing in the world are finite. The author states that the types of each division (specific to animals and non-specific) are finite in existence. He provides two proofs: induction (empirical observation) and the proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq), a logical proof often used to demonstrate finiteness of a set. The commentator clarifies that the text's statement about each division being finite does not directly state that the total number of accidental types is finite, but since the divisions themselves are finite, the total also follows as finite.

Infinite Types of Accidents in Potentiality
Question about infinite types of accidents in potentiality with disagreement noted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether infinite types of accidents are possible in potentiality and the key clarification that the discussion concerns potential existence, not actual accidents.

Core Idea
Can there be infinite possible types of accidents? The Mu'tazila say no.

This passage raises a subtle question about the scope of possibility: could there be an infinite number of distinct accidental natures that could potentially exist, even if only a finite number actually come into being? The author notes the disagreement and clarifies the meaning of potential accidents, emphasizing that existence is part of the definition of accident for the verifiers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice how the passage moves from the question to the commentator's clarification.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage asks whether infinite types of accidents are possible. It then states that most Mu'tazila and many others deny this possibility. Identify the opposing view (the one who affirms is not named but implied).
3
Understand the commentator's clarification
The commentator explains that the potential accidents in question are such that if they existed, they would be accidents; they are not accidents in the state of pure potentiality. This is because existence is part of the definition of accident according to the verifiers.
This clarification distinguishes between logical possibility and actual accidental properties.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this matters in kalam: limiting or affirming infinite possibilities relates to discussions about God's power and the nature of creation. The Mu'tazila's denial likely stems from their view on the nature of accidents.
This connects to broader debates about contingency and the created world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل يمكن أن منه ) أي من العرض ( أنواع غير متناهية ) بأن يكون في الإمكان وجود أعراض نوعية مغايرة للأعراض المعهودة إلى غير النهاية وإن لم يخرج منها إلى الوجود إلا ما هو متناه أو لا يمكن ذلك اختلف فيه فمن (منعه وهم أكثر المعتزلة وكثير من

قوله : ( بأن يكون في الإمكان وجود أعراض إلخ) بمعنى أنها لو وجدت لكانت عرضاً، لا أنها أعراض في مرتبة الإمكان الصرف؛ لأن الوجود مأخوذ في تعريف العرض عند أهل التحقيق.
And can there be from it (i.e., from accident) infinite types, that is, could there be in potentiality accidental types different from the usual accidents ad infinitum, even if only a finite amount emerges into existence? Or is that not possible? There is disagreement on it; so some deny it, and they are most of the Mu'tazila and many of ... His saying: ('that could be in potentiality the existence of accidents etc.') meaning that if they existed they would be accidents, not that they are accidents in the rank of pure potentiality because existence is taken into account in the definition of accident according to the verifiers.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage raises the question of whether there can be infinite types of accidents in potentiality, even if only a finite number actually exist. The author notes that there is disagreement: the majority of the Mu'tazila and many others deny the possibility. The commentator clarifies the meaning of the statement: the potential accidents are such that if they were to exist, they would be accidents; they are not actually accidents in the state of pure potentiality, because for the verifiers (ahl al-tahqiq), existence is part of the definition of accident. Thus, the discussion is about whether an infinite number of distinct accidental natures could be possible, not about actually existing infinite accidents.

Key Terms

الإمكان
الأعراض النوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The discussion concerns actual infinite types of accidents that exist simultaneously.
Correction: It concerns potential infinite types in possibility—accidental natures that could exist, not that do exist. Only a finite number are actualized.

Checkpoint

B) They deny it

Key Takeaway
The possibility of infinite types of accidents in potentiality is debated; most Mu'tazila deny it, and the key clarification is that the discussion concerns potential existence, not actual accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage clarifies that the discussion is about potentiality, not actuality. In your own spiritual life, are you more concerned with what is actualized or with what is potential? How might preoccupation with potentiality distract you from acting on what you can control today?
?
If scholars disagree on whether infinite types of accidents can even be potential, how should that inform your attitude towards theological debates? Are you quick to claim certainty on matters where scholars differ, or do you approach such questions with humility? What specific step can you take this week to cultivate a humble posture before complex theological truths?

The Debate on Whether the Akwan Are Sensible
Presents the disagreement on whether the four akwan are sensible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among scholars about whether the four akwan (motion, rest, union, separation) are directly perceived by the senses or only inferred from perceiving their bearers.

Core Idea
The akwan are not directly sensed; we only sense the objects that are in these states.

This passage introduces a key debate in kalam: are the 'states' (akwan) of motion, rest, union, and separation themselves sensible? Some theologians argue they are necessarily perceived by sight, while others contend we only see the moving or resting objects, not the states themselves. Understanding this disagreement is crucial for grasping later discussions on the existence and nature of these states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to grasp the two opposing views on whether the akwan are sensible.
Pay attention to the arguments each side uses.
2
Identify the two positions
Note the first group's claim that akwan are necessarily sensible and the second group's claim that they are not, along with their supporting reasoning.
The second group argues we only perceive moving objects, not the motion itself.
3
Examine the commentary
Read the commentator's explanation, which clarifies the phrase 'necessarily sensible' and points out the confusion between direct and indirect perception.
The commentator also notes that even sensible things like colors have been denied by some.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب بعضهم إلى أن الأكوان محسوسة بالضرورة ومن أنكر الأكوان فقد كابر حسه ومقتضى عقله، وآخرون إلى أنها غير محسوسة فإنَّا لا نشاهد إلا المتحرك الساكن والمجتمعين والمفترقين وأما وصف الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق فلا ولهذا اختلف في كونها وجودية ولو كانت محسوسة لما وقع الخلاف فيها . قوله : (محسوسة بالضرورة) أي بالبصر فهي داخلة في المحسوسات ومنشأ هذا القول عدم الفرق بين المحسوس بالذات وبين المحسوس بالواسطة . قوله : ( لما وقع الخلاف إذ لا شبهة في وجود المحسوسات وإن كابره منكرو الحسيات . قوله : ( لما وقع الخلاف فيها أي بين كثيرين، وإلا فسيجيء أن بعضاً من القدماء قال : لا وجود للألوان مع أنها محسوسة .
And some went to that the akwan are necessarily sensible, and whoever denies the akwan has defied his sense and the requirement of his intellect. And others (went to) that they are not sensible, for we only observe the moving, the resting, the two together, and the two separate; but as for the description of motion, rest, union, and separation, no. And therefore there is disagreement over their being existent. And if they were sensible, there would have been no disagreement about them. His saying: (necessarily sensible) i.e., by sight, so they are included among sensible things. The origin of this statement is the lack of distinction between the sensible in essence and the sensible indirectly. His saying: (for disagreement occurred) for there is no doubt about the existence of sensible things, even if deniers of the sensible contradict them. His saying: (for disagreement occurred in them) i.e., among many; otherwise it will come that some of the ancients said: colors have no existence even though they are sensible.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute regarding whether the four akwan (states of motion, rest, union, separation) are sensible by the senses. Some scholars assert that they are necessarily sensible by sight, thus included among sensible things. They argue that denying them contradicts both sense and reason. Others hold that they are not sensible because we only perceive the objects that are moving, resting, together, or separate, but we do not directly perceive the attributes of motion, rest, etc. This disagreement leads to a further dispute about whether these states have real existence. The commentary clarifies that the claim of necessary sensibility arises from not distinguishing between direct and indirect perception. It also notes that disagreement exists, and even colors—which are sensible—have been denied existence by some.

Key Terms
أكوان (akwan) states
محسوس بالضرورة (mahsus bil-darura) necessarily sensible
محسوس بالذات (mahsus bil-dhat) sensible in essence
محسوس بالواسطة (mahsus bil-wasita) sensible indirectly

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might assume that the akwan (states) are directly perceived by the senses, just like their bearers.
Correction: The passage clarifies that we only perceive the objects (e.g., a moving body) but not the abstract state of motion itself; the akwan are inferred, not directly sensed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do some deny that akwan are sensible?
A) Because they are not physical
B) Because we perceive only the moving objects, not the motion itself
C) Because they are invisible
D) Because they are abstract

Key Takeaway
The four akwan are not directly sensible; rather, their existence is inferred from the observable objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you mistaken something you inferred for something you directly observed? How does this lesson's distinction between 'sensible in essence' and 'sensible indirectly' challenge your everyday confidence in your senses? Will you pause to question your perceptions more often?
?
The ancients denied the existence of colors even though they are sensible. What is something you firmly believe to be true because it 'seems obvious'? Are you willing to examine that belief against the possibility that you are conflating direct perception with inference? What practical step can you take today to test one such belief?
?
You cannot see motion itself, only moving things. In your spiritual life, what 'invisible' realities (like intention, gratitude, or faith) do you treat as merely inferred rather than as real as physical objects? How would your day change if you gave those realities the same certainty you give to a moving car?

Infinite Accident Types and the Doctrine of Suspension
Debate on infinite accident types with suspension as verdict

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological debate on whether infinite types of accidents are possible and why the verifiers adopt suspension (tawaqquf).

Core Idea
Infinite accident types: a puzzle resolved by suspension due to weak arguments.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a classic dispute in kalam: can there be an infinite number of distinct accident types in potentiality? The author reviews arguments for denial and permission, then reveals the position of the verifiers—suspension—due to the weakness of both proofs. The commentary clarifies key terms and refines the objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: question, two opposing views, and the verdict of suspension.
The passage includes both the main argument and commentary clarifications.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Find the denial argument (most Mu'tazila and Ash'aris) based on 'every number is subject to increase and decrease, so it is finite' and the proof of application. Then find the permission argument (al-Jubba'i and others) that 'no number is more entitled than another, so infinite can exist'.
The denial relies on the notion that infinite cannot be increased; the permission relies on the absence of a reason to stop.
3
Understand the proof of application
The proof of application (burhan al-tatbiq) is invoked by the deniers. It requires that all individuals actually exist to compare them. The commentary explains that if they do not all exist, the proof fails.
This proof is central to the debate and its condition is discussed in detail.
4
Grasp why suspension is the correct position
The verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) suspend judgment because both arguments are weak: the denial argument's proof requires actual existence which may not hold, and the permission argument leads to infinite regress or other issues. The passage ends with an invitation to reflect.
Suspension here means not committing to either denial or permission until further evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل يمكن أن منه ) أي من العرض ( أنواع غير متناهية ) بأن يكون في الإمكان وجود أعراض نوعية مغايرة للأعراض المعهودة إلى غير النهاية وإن لم يخرج منها إلى الوجود إلا ما هو متناه أو لا يمكن ذلك اختلف فيه فمن (منعه وهم أكثر المعتزلة وكثير من الأشاعرة ( نظر إلى أن كل عدد قابل للزيادة والنقصان ) فهو متناه ) لأن ما لا يتناهى لا يكون قابلاً لهما وللتطبيق أيضاً ( ومن جوزه ( كالجبائي وأتباعه والقاضي منافي أكثر أجوبته ( فلأنه ليس عدد أولى من عدد فوجد اللاتناهي ( كما مر والحق ) عند المحققين (هو التوقف) وعدم الجزم بالمنع أو الجواز لضعف المأخذين ووجهه ) أي وجه ضعفهما ( ظاهر) . قوله : ( قابل للزيادة والنقصان ) بأن يزيد بعد أن كان ناقصاً ذكر النقصان استطرادي إنما المنافي للاتناهي قبوله للزيادة . قوله : (لا ينافي عدم التناهي) أي الذي كلامنا فيه أي بمعنى أن لا يقف عند حد وإن كان منافياً لعدم التناهي بالفعل. قوله فيما ضبطه وجود أي دخل تحت الوجود جميع أفراده ليمكن التطبيق بين آحاده في نفس الأمر فيلزم المحال كما مر تفصيله. قوله : ( غير متناهية ) أي غير منقطعة بناء على عدم انقطاع نعيم أهل الجنة وعذاب أهل النار. قوله : ( ومن أن برهان التطبيق لا يتم إلا فيما ضبطه وجود فيه بحث لأنَّ الظاهر أنَّ مراد المنكر لإمكان غير المتناهي من الأنواع أنه لو أمكن لم يلزم من وجوده محال واللازم باطل لأنه على تقدير وجوده يجري فيه برهان التطبيق لعدم اشتراط الترتب فيه عند المتكلمين كما سبق وحينئذ يلزم أحد المحالين إما مساواة الناقص الزائد أو تناهي ما فرض غير متناه فلا برهان التطبيق لا يتم إلا فيما ضبطه وجود لأنَّ الكلام على تقدير الوجود وأما تجويزهم عدم تناهي الأفراد الممكنة لكل نوع، فينبغي أن يحمل على تجويز كل درجة؛ لا إلى نهاية وإمكان كل درجة في أنفسها؛ لا ينافي استحالة الكل لبطلان التسلسل في الأنواع فيتأتى التوفيق فتأمل .
And can there be from it (i.e., from the accident) infinite types—meaning that it is possible for there to exist specific accidents different from the usual accidents ad infinitum, even if only what is finite comes into existence from them—or is that not possible? There is disagreement about it. Those who deny it—and they are most of the Mu'tazila and many of the Ash'aris—argue that every number is subject to increase and decrease, so it is finite; because that which is infinite cannot be subject to them, and also by the proof of application. And those who permit it—like al-Jubba'i, his followers, and al-Qadi, contrary to most of his answers—argue that no number is more entitled than another, so infinite comes to be found, as has passed. And the truth according to the verifiers is suspension and not being certain of denial or permission, due to the weakness of the two grounds, and its explanation (i.e., the explanation of their weakness) is apparent. His saying: (subject to increase and decrease) meaning it increases after having been deficient; the mention of decrease is incidental; rather, what opposes infiniteness is its acceptance of increase. His saying: (does not contradict non-infiniteness) i.e., that which we are discussing, i.e., in the sense that it does not stop at a limit, even if it contradicts actual non-infiniteness. His saying: (in what existence encompasses) i.e., all its individuals enter under existence so that application can be made between its units in reality, and then impossibility follows, as its detail has passed. His saying: (non-finite) i.e., uninterrupted, based on the uninterruptedness of the bliss of the people of Paradise and the punishment of the people of Hell. His saying: (And that the proof of application is only complete in what existence encompasses, there is discussion about it, because apparently the intention of the denier of the possibility of infinite types is that if it were possible, no impossibility would follow from its existence, and the necessary is false, because on the assumption of its existence, the proof of application applies to it due to the absence of the condition of ordering according to the mutakallimun, as has preceded. Then one of two impossibilities would follow: either the equality of the deficient and the exceeding, or the finitude of what was assumed infinite. Thus the proof of application is only complete in what existence encompasses, because the discussion is on the assumption of existence. As for their permitting the non-finiteness of possible individuals of each type, it should be interpreted as permission of each degree, not to an end, and the possibility of each degree in itself does not contradict the impossibility of the whole due to the invalidity of regress in types. So reconciliation is attained. So reflect.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a detailed dialectical argument. It presents the question of whether there can be infinite types of accidents in potentiality. Some deny it (most Mu'tazila and many Ash'aris) arguing that every number is subject to increase and decrease, so it must be finite, and the proof of application also shows impossibility. Others permit it (al-Jubba'i, his followers, and al-Qadi) because no number is prior to another, so infinite is possible. The truth according to the verifiers is tawaqquf (suspension) due to the weakness of both arguments. The commentary then refutes both sides in detail: it explains that acceptance of increase does not negate non-finiteness, as in doubling one infinitely; and the proof of application only applies if all individuals are actually realized. It also discusses the issue of infinite individuals in paradise and hell, concluding that the possibility of infinite individuals per type does not necessitate infinite types, thus reconciliation is possible. The passage ends with an invitation to reflect.

Key Terms
غير متناهية (ghayr mutanahiya) infinite
توقف (tawaqquf) suspension
برهان التطبيق (burhan al-tatbiq) proof of application
قابل للزيادة والنقصان (qabil lil-ziyada wal-nuqsan) subject to increase and decrease

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of application always works to disprove infinite types.
Correction: The proof of application only works if all individuals actually exist; if they are only potential, the proof does not apply, as explained in the commentary.

Checkpoint
What is the correct position according to the verifiers regarding the possibility of infinite types of accidents?
A) Denial
B) Permission
C) Suspension
D) Affirmation

Key Takeaway
The verifiers suspend judgment on infinite accident types due to the weakness of both the denial and permission arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are faced with two opposing views that both have weak evidence, do you have the patience and humility to suspend judgment, or do you feel pressured to pick a side? What would it take for you to honestly say 'I don't know' in matters of your faith?
?
The deniers relied on a proof that only works under specific conditions (actual existence). Have you ever held a strong belief that you later discovered was based on an unexamined assumption? How does this example challenge you to examine the foundations of your certainties?
?
The passage mentions infinite bliss and punishment. Are you comfortable with theological questions that remain unresolved, or do you need definitive answers to feel secure? What does this lesson teach you about trusting the verifiers who embrace uncertainty over false certainty?
