Defining Quantity: The Two Meanings of Acceptance of Division
Introduces classification of accidents and defines quantity via two senses of division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of 'acceptance of division' in the definition of quantity and why each is problematic when used alone.

Core Idea
Quantity is defined by division, but 'division' can mean imagining parts or actual separation.

In this passage, the author begins the classification of accidents and defines quantity as that which accepts division by itself. He then introduces two meanings of 'acceptance of division' from al-Mulakhkhas: one is the potential to have parts imagined (applies to magnitude), and the other is actual separation into new identities (applies to matter, not magnitude). This distinction is crucial because it reveals that a single definition cannot cover both continuous and discrete quantities unless carefully worded.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification and definition
Start by reading how the philosophers divide accidents: those that accept division by themselves (quantity) and those that do not. Note the reason for adding 'by itself'—to exclude accidental quantity like knowledge.
2
Understand the two meanings of division
The passage then presents two meanings from al-Mulakhkhas: (1) something else can be imagined in it (imaginal division) and (2) actual separation creating two identities. Compare the two meanings and see how they apply to magnitude and matter respectively.
Imaginal division applies to magnitude; actual separation does not, because magnitude ceases when divided.
3
See why the definition fails
The author notes that if we define quantity only by 'acceptance of division', it becomes specific to continuous quantity because the second meaning (actual separation) does not apply to magnitude. This sets up the problem for the next lesson.
This shows that 'acceptance of division' is ambiguous and needs refinement.
4
Reflect on the induction note
Consider the brief remark about induction: the definition is meant to facilitate induction by narrowing the focus to the last part of the division. This is a methodological point about how to study categories.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المعنى يلحق المقدار لذاته فإنه لم یورده بطريق الحصر ليستفاد منه اختصاصه بالمتصل وما قيل : إِن قوله بل القابل للانقسام بهذا المعنى هو المادة يدل على عدم لحوقه للمنفصل فضلا عن اختصاصه حيث أورده بطريق الحصر فكيف يكون كلامه السابق مقتضياً لاختصاصه بالمنفصل فجوابه أنَّ القصر إضافي بالنسبة إلى كلامه. قوله : ( نوع إشعار إلخ لأن لحوقه للمقدار لذاته مع أن جزء المقدار مقدار يدل على عدم انقطاعه لكن لما كان الإشعار محتاجاً إلى ضم مقدمة قال : نوع إشعار إشارة إلى خفائه . قوله : (بل هو شامل إلخ إضراب عما يستفاد من الكلام السابق أي فليس مختصاً بالمتصل . قوله : ( ولا يزال كذلك أبداً كأنه حمل المضارع أعني قوله أن يفرض على الاستمرار والتجدد الدائمي ثم إنَّ المراد أن يكون هذا المعنى لازماً بحسب كل جزء وقسمة فلا يرد النقض بأجزاء العدد الغير المتناهي كعدد النفوس المفارقة عند الفلاسفة مثلاً فإنه يقبل القسمة لا إلى نهاية لكنه بحسب بعض الأجزاء والتجزئة والاختصاص الحد بالمتصل وجه آخر وهو أن يحل العرض المذكور في تفسير القسمة على المتبادر وهو المقابل للفعل فيخرج المنفصل حينئذ لأنه منقسم بالفعل البتة ويمكن أن يخرج المنفصل بالقبول أيضاً بأن يراد به الإمكان المقابل للفعل. قوله : ( نوع إشعار بهذا القيد حيث قيل إلخ) وجه الإشعار أنه إذا لم يعتبر هذا القيد يكون
First observatory - Third objective: Accident (al-‘arad) and its divisions according to the philosophers. [Text continues] The ten possible existents, but they did not bring in the enumeration what is reliable, and their reliance in proving the enumeration is incomplete induction. The way to control it so that it reduces dispersion and facilitates induction is that they said: The accident either accepts division by itself or not, and the first is quantity (al-kamm). We only said 'by itself' to exclude quantity by accident, like knowledge of two things, for it is divisible but not by itself, but by its relation to the two known things that are subjects of number; and later you will see the divisions of quantity by accident. And what is meant by division here, i.e., in the definition of quantity, is that something else be imagined in it, so it includes continuous (al-muttasil) and discrete (al-munfasil), because both are divisible in this sense. And it is mentioned in al-Mulakhkhas that acceptance of division may mean: the thing being such that something else can be imagined in it, and this meaning attaches to magnitude by itself; and it may mean: separation such that two identities occur for the body, and this meaning does not attach to magnitude because what is followed must remain when the follower comes, and when one magnitude separates it ceases to exist and two magnitudes occur that did not exist actually before separation; rather, what is divisible in this sense is matter, and magnitude in its acceptance of it. Then it is mentioned there that it is not permissible to define quantity by acceptance of division because it is specific to the continuous. His statement: 'Either it accepts by itself division' meaning it is a substrate for it without mediation of another thing. His statement: 'Divisions of quantity by accident' and it is the locus of quantity by itself or the state in it or the state in its locus or [complete]. His statement: 'Because each of them etc.' and that parts are actually existent does not contradict imagining them, rather it helps. His statement: 'It occurs for the body' he specified body to indicate that this division attaches to body by itself because motion is necessary in it, and it only attaches to accidents by way of following. His statement: 'It is not permissible to define quantity etc.' In al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, some of them restricted the definition of quantity to acceptance of equality and inequality, and some added acceptance of division to it, and that is a mistake, for acceptance of division is an accident of continuous quantity, not of discrete quantity, unless acceptance is taken by homonymy. End of quote. And what is derived from it is that it is not permissible to define it by acceptance of division absolutely, and that the source of incorrectness is the word 'acceptance' and its interpretation: if what is meant is imagining and attribution, and by division is meant imaginative division, since separation only occurs in matter, then it is specific to continuous, not discrete; either because 'imaginative' is only applied as opposed to intellectual, as they stated in the definition of part, or because of consideration of the condition of non-interruption in it; and if what is meant is occurrence and by division is meant separative division, because [it is]... His statement: 'And it facilitates induction' It is said: the way to facilitate induction is that the sender of the division is only the last part, so induction is specific to it; one investigates whether multiple instances of it exist or not; and there is no need for induction in the remaining judgments, because they are obtained by mental disjunction. In it: And it is not hidden from you that what his previous statement requires is that if quantity is defined by acceptance of division and what is meant by it is separation, it would not include the continuous but would be specific to the discrete; but since he explicitly stated that the definition is specific to the continuous, it is necessary that the first meaning be intended and that a condition be intended in it, as al-Katibi did in his commentary, where he said, quoting from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: One of the two meanings is that it be such that something else can be imagined in it, and it continues like that forever, and there is no doubt that this condition makes it specific to the continuous, because the unit into which the discrete is divided cannot have something else imagined in it. And in the expression of al-Mulakhkhas there is a kind of indication of this condition, where it was said: 'And this meaning attaches to magnitude by itself.' But the correct view is that this addition is not considered in the first meaning; rather, it includes both continuous and discrete together. And the author indicated this by his statement: 'So the statement of Imam al-Razi is not rebutted...' and also because separation only occurs in the continuous, so the definition by acceptance of division is not valid unless acceptance is taken in both meanings by way of homonymy, either by using the homonym in both meanings or by intending the common denominator, and by division is meant separative division; then the definition includes both continuous and discrete, because the occurrence of separation is for the discrete and its origination is on the continuous. This is the gist of the Imam's words in the two books, in my view. His statement: 'Indeed, what his previous statement requires' and it is his statement: 'And this meaning does not attach to magnitude'; for the negation of its attaching to magnitude indicates its non-inclusion, unlike his statement: 'And this meaning attaches to magnitude by itself', for he did not present it as a restriction to deduce its specificity to the continuous. And what is said: that his statement 'Rather, what is divisible in this sense is matter' indicates its non-attachment to the discrete, let alone its specificity, since he presented it as a restriction, so how can his previous statement require its specificity to the discrete? The answer is that the restriction is relative with respect to his statement. His statement: 'A kind of indication etc.' because its attaching to magnitude by itself, while part of magnitude is magnitude, indicates its non-interruption; but since the indication needs an added premise, he said 'a kind of indication' as a sign of its subtlety. His statement: 'Rather, it is inclusive etc.' is a digression from what is derived from the previous statement, i.e., it is not specific to the continuous. His statement: 'And it continues like that forever' as if he construed the present tense verb, i.e., his saying 'that something be imagined', as denoting continuous and perpetual renewal. Then what is meant is that this meaning is a necessary concomitant of every part and division; so the objection by infinite parts of number, like the number of separate souls according to the philosophers, for example, is not rebutted; for it accepts division ad infinitum, but only with respect to some parts and partition. And the specificity of the definition to the continuous has another aspect: that the accident mentioned in the interpretation of division be resolved on the obvious meaning, which is the opposite of actual, thus the discrete is excluded because it is actually divided. And the discrete can also be excluded by acceptance if what is meant by it is possibility as opposed to actual. His statement: 'A kind of indication of this condition where it was said etc.' The indication is that if this condition is not considered, it would be...
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical discussion on the definition of accident (al-‘arad) and specifically the category of quantity (al-kamm) according to the philosophers. The author begins by critiquing the enumeration of ten categories of accidents, noting that their reliance on incomplete induction is flawed. He then focuses on the definition of quantity. The main argument: Accidents are divided into those that accept division by themselves and those that do not. The first is quantity. 'By itself' excludes accidental quantity like knowledge that is divisible due to its objects not its essence. Division here means that something else can be imagined in it—this applies to both continuous (e.g., a line) and discrete (e.g., number) quantities. The author then cites al-Mulakhkhas which distinguishes two meanings of 'acceptance of division': (1) the thing can be imagined as having parts (attaches to magnitude essentially), and (2) actual separation where a body gains two identities (attaches to matter, not magnitude). Because magnitude when separated ceases to exist, it is not divisible in the second sense. Therefore, defining quantity solely by acceptance of division is problematic since it is specific to continuous quantities. The commentary then discusses objections and clarifications. It addresses the validity of defining quantity by acceptance of equality and inequality instead, and the view that acceptance of division is an accident of continuous quantity only, unless taken by homonymy. It explains that if 'acceptance' means imaginative division, it is specific to continuous because imaginative division doesn't apply to discrete units like numbers. If it means actual separation, it is also specific to continuous because separation is an accident of continuous quantities. The induction note clarifies that the last part of the division is what is investigated. The core of the lesson: The definition of quantity must be carefully worded to cover both continuous and discrete, and the solution is to take the term 'acceptance' by homonymy to include both meanings. The author then discusses al-Katibi's addition of the condition 'forever' to restrict definition to continuous, but argues this is unnecessary and that the correct view is that the first meaning (imaginal division) already includes both. He concludes by summarizing al-Razi's view: definition by acceptance of division is valid only if acceptance is taken in both senses via homonymy, allowing inclusion of both continuous and discrete. The passage ends with an explanation of the subtlety of the indication in al-Mulakhkhas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'acceptance of division' is a simple, unambiguous property that straightforwardly defines quantity.
Correction: The passage shows that 'acceptance of division' has two distinct meanings (imaginal and separative), and neither alone covers both continuous and discrete quantities, so the definition must be carefully handled.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'knowledge of two things' not a quantity by essence?
A) Because knowledge is not an accident.
B) Because it is divisible due to its objects, not itself.
C) Because it is not divisible at all.
D) Because knowledge is a substance.

Key Takeaway
Quantity is defined by accepting division, but 'division' has two senses—imaginal (for magnitude) and actual separation (for matter)—and using either alone fails to cover both continuous and discrete quantities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time when you mentally divided a problem or situation into 'parts'. Did that division help you understand it better? How does the concept of 'imaginal division' (al-fard) apply to the way you analyze your own beliefs and actions? Commit to examining one belief you hold by mentally dissecting its components this week.
?
The passage distinguishes between division that preserves the thing (imaginal) and division that destroys it (actual). Are there relationships or habits in your life that, if 'divided' or separated, would cease to exist in their current form? What would you lose if you actually separated from them? Write one concrete step to protect a relationship or habit that you value.
?
You learned that quantity accepts division 'by itself', not due to an external factor. Consider your own sense of self: is it defined by what you inherently are, or by external factors like your job, status, or possessions? Identify one aspect of your identity that you rely on that is actually accidental. How can you ground your identity more in what is essential (like your faith) rather than in what can be divided away?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
5 min
Resolving the Definition: Homonymy and the Inclusive Definition
Presents the solution to the definition problem via homonymy and discusses al-Katibi's alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the problem of defining quantity is resolved by taking 'acceptance of division' as a homonym covering both imaginative and separative division, or by adding a condition to include both continuous and discrete.

Core Idea
A proper definition of quantity uses 'acceptance' in two senses via homonymy.

In the previous lesson, we saw that defining quantity solely by 'acceptance of division' fails because the term is ambiguous. This passage presents the solution: the only way to include both continuous and discrete quantities is to take 'acceptance' in both meanings—imaginative division (for continuous) and separative division (for discrete)—by using the word as a homonym. The author also discusses an alternative approach by al-Katibi that adds the condition 'forever' to restrict to continuous, but argues it is unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and its basis
Read the opening line: 'So the statement of Imam al-Razi is not rebutted...' This refers to the claim that definition by acceptance of division is invalid unless acceptance is taken in both meanings. Identify why a single meaning fails.
2
Understand the solution of homonymy
The passage explains that 'acceptance' can be used in two different ways: one for imaginative division (applies to continuous) and one for separative division (applies to discrete). Using the word in both senses (homonymy) allows the definition to cover both.
This is like using 'bank' to mean both a financial institution and a river bank.
3
Consider al-Katibi's condition
Al-Katibi added the condition 'and it continues like that forever' to restrict the definition to continuous quantities. The author argues this is unnecessary because the first meaning already includes both.
Think about whether adding 'forever' changes the coverage.
4
Reflect on the author's conclusion
The author ends by summarizing al-Razi's view: the definition works if acceptance is taken as a homonym. See how this solves the earlier problem.

First observatory - Third objective: Accident (al-‘arad) and its divisions according to the philosophers. [Text continues] The ten possible existents, but they did not bring in the enumeration what is reliable, and their reliance in proving the enumeration is incomplete induction. The way to control it so that it reduces dispersion and facilitates induction is that they said: The accident either accepts division by itself or not, and the first is quantity (al-kamm). We only said 'by itself' to exclude quantity by accident, like knowledge of two things, for it is divisible but not by itself, but by its relation to the two known things that are subjects of number; and later you will see the divisions of quantity by accident. And what is meant by division here, i.e., in the definition of quantity, is that something else be imagined in it, so it includes continuous (al-muttasil) and discrete (al-munfasil), because both are divisible in this sense. And it is mentioned in al-Mulakhkhas that acceptance of division may mean: the thing being such that something else can be imagined in it, and this meaning attaches to magnitude by itself; and it may mean: separation such that two identities occur for the body, and this meaning does not attach to magnitude because what is followed must remain when the follower comes, and when one magnitude separates it ceases to exist and two magnitudes occur that did not exist actually before separation; rather, what is divisible in this sense is matter, and magnitude in its acceptance of it. Then it is mentioned there that it is not permissible to define quantity by acceptance of division because it is specific to the continuous. His statement: 'Either it accepts by itself division' meaning it is a substrate for it without mediation of another thing. His statement: 'Divisions of quantity by accident' and it is the locus of quantity by itself or the state in it or the state in its locus or [complete]. His statement: 'Because each of them etc.' and that parts are actually existent does not contradict imagining them, rather it helps. His statement: 'It occurs for the body' he specified body to indicate that this division attaches to body by itself because motion is necessary in it, and it only attaches to accidents by way of following. His statement: 'It is not permissible to define quantity etc.' In al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, some of them restricted the definition of quantity to acceptance of equality and inequality, and some added acceptance of division to it, and that is a mistake, for acceptance of division is an accident of continuous quantity, not of discrete quantity, unless acceptance is taken by homonymy. End of quote. And what is derived from it is that it is not permissible to define it by acceptance of division absolutely, and that the source of incorrectness is the word 'acceptance' and its interpretation: if what is meant is imagining and attribution, and by division is meant imaginative division, since separation only occurs in matter, then it is specific to continuous, not discrete; either because 'imaginative' is only applied as opposed to intellectual, as they stated in the definition of part, or because of consideration of the condition of non-interruption in it; and if what is meant is occurrence and by division is meant separative division, because [it is]... His statement: 'And it facilitates induction' It is said: the way to facilitate induction is that the sender of the division is only the last part, so induction is specific to it; one investigates whether multiple instances of it exist or not; and there is no need for induction in the remaining judgments, because they are obtained by mental disjunction. In it: And it is not hidden from you that what his previous statement requires is that if quantity is defined by acceptance of division and what is meant by it is separation, it would not include the continuous but would be specific to the discrete; but since he explicitly stated that the definition is specific to the continuous, it is necessary that the first meaning be intended and that a condition be intended in it, as al-Katibi did in his commentary, where he said, quoting from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: One of the two meanings is that it be such that something else can be imagined in it, and it continues like that forever, and there is no doubt that this condition makes it specific to the continuous, because the unit into which the discrete is divided cannot have something else imagined in it. And in the expression of al-Mulakhkhas there is a kind of indication of this condition, where it was said: 'And this meaning attaches to magnitude by itself.' But the correct view is that this addition is not considered in the first meaning; rather, it includes both continuous and discrete together. And the author indicated this by his statement: 'So the statement of Imam al-Razi is not rebutted...' and also because separation only occurs in the continuous, so the definition by acceptance of division is not valid unless acceptance is taken in both meanings by way of homonymy, either by using the homonym in both meanings or by intending the common denominator, and by division is meant separative division; then the definition includes both continuous and discrete, because the occurrence of separation is for the discrete and its origination is on the continuous. This is the gist of the Imam's words in the two books, in my view. His statement: 'Indeed, what his previous statement requires' and it is his statement: 'And this meaning does not attach to magnitude'; for the negation of its attaching to magnitude indicates its non-inclusion, unlike his statement: 'And this meaning attaches to magnitude by itself', for he did not present it as a restriction to deduce its specificity to the continuous. And what is said: that his statement 'Rather, what is divisible in this sense is matter' indicates its non-attachment to the discrete, let alone its specificity, since he presented it as a restriction, so how can his previous statement require its specificity to the discrete? The answer is that the restriction is relative with respect to his statement. His statement: 'A kind of indication etc.' because its attaching to magnitude by itself, while part of magnitude is magnitude, indicates its non-interruption; but since the indication needs an added premise, he said 'a kind of indication' as a sign of its subtlety. His statement: 'Rather, it is inclusive etc.' is a digression from what is derived from the previous statement, i.e., it is not specific to the continuous. His statement: 'And it continues like that forever' as if he construed the present tense verb, i.e., his saying 'that something be imagined', as denoting continuous and perpetual renewal. Then what is meant is that this meaning is a necessary concomitant of every part and division; so the objection by infinite parts of number, like the number of separate souls according to the philosophers, for example, is not rebutted; for it accepts division ad infinitum, but only with respect to some parts and partition. And the specificity of the definition to the continuous has another aspect: that the accident mentioned in the interpretation of division be resolved on the obvious meaning, which is the opposite of actual, thus the discrete is excluded because it is actually divided. And the discrete can also be excluded by acceptance if what is meant by it is possibility as opposed to actual. His statement: 'A kind of indication of this condition where it was said etc.' The indication is that if this condition is not considered, it would be...
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical discussion on the definition of accident (al-‘arad) and specifically the category of quantity (al-kamm) according to the philosophers. The author begins by critiquing the enumeration of ten categories of accidents, noting that their reliance on incomplete induction is flawed. He then focuses on the definition of quantity. The main argument: Accidents are divided into those that accept division by themselves and those that do not. The first is quantity. 'By itself' excludes accidental quantity like knowledge that is divisible due to its objects not its essence. Division here means that something else can be imagined in it—this applies to both continuous (e.g., a line) and discrete (e.g., number) quantities. The author then cites al-Mulakhkhas which distinguishes two meanings of 'acceptance of division': (1) the thing can be imagined as having parts (attaches to magnitude essentially), and (2) actual separation where a body gains two identities (attaches to matter, not magnitude). Because magnitude when separated ceases to exist, it is not divisible in the second sense. Therefore, defining quantity solely by acceptance of division is problematic since it is specific to continuous quantities. The commentary then discusses objections and clarifications. It addresses the validity of defining quantity by acceptance of equality and inequality instead, and the view that acceptance of division is an accident of continuous quantity only, unless taken by homonymy. It explains that if 'acceptance' means imaginative division, it is specific to continuous because imaginative division doesn't apply to discrete units like numbers. If it means actual separation, it is also specific to continuous because separation is an accident of continuous quantities. The induction note clarifies that the last part of the division is what is investigated. The core of the lesson: The definition of quantity must be carefully worded to cover both continuous and discrete, and the solution is to take the term 'acceptance' by homonymy to include both meanings. The author then discusses al-Katibi's addition of the condition 'forever' to restrict definition to continuous, but argues this is unnecessary and that the correct view is that the first meaning (imaginal division) already includes both. He concludes by summarizing al-Razi's view: definition by acceptance of division is valid only if acceptance is taken in both senses via homonymy, allowing inclusion of both continuous and discrete. The passage ends with an explanation of the subtlety of the indication in al-Mulakhkhas.

Key Terms
الاشتراك
الخارجة
الطارئة
الفرض
الافتراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the only way to define quantity is to abandon division altogether and use another property like equality/inequality.
Correction: The passage shows that by using homonymy, the definition by division can be preserved and cover both types of quantity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does using 'acceptance' as a homonym solve the definition problem?
A) Because it allows the definition to apply to both imaginary and separative division.
B) Because it makes the definition simpler.
C) Because it excludes discrete quantities.
D) Because it was the only option accepted by all philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The problem of defining quantity by acceptance of division is resolved by using 'acceptance' as a homonym, allowing the definition to include both continuous and discrete quantities via its two distinct senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson shows that a term like 'acceptance' can have two distinct applications. In your own understanding of faith, are there terms you define too narrowly, causing unnecessary doubts or divisions? Commit to examining one word in your spiritual vocabulary that you might be using inflexibly.
?
The author uses homonymy to rescue a definition that seemed to fail. The Real Definition of Al-Ayn (Where)
Sanusi defines the real 'ayn as the first relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the real definition of al-ayn (where) as the first of the seven relations and why it is considered a real 'where'.

Core Idea
Al-ayn is the body's obtaining in its own proper space.

Before exploring the broader categories of relations in kalam, we must understand the first type: al-ayn, or 'where'. This lesson introduces the real meaning of 'where' as the body's presence in its specific place. The passage from Sanusi defines this precise concept and sets the stage for further discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice that the definition involves the body's 'obtaining' (ḥuṣūl) in a place.
The passage is concise; read slowly.
2
Understand the real definition
The passage states that real 'ayn is the body's occupation of its own space. Pay attention to the phrase 'in the space that belongs to it and is filled by it'."
3
Consider the second definition
An alternative definition is given: 'a quality that obtains for the body due to its relation to its real place'. Think about how this differs from the first.
This second definition is debated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والأول ) وهو ما يكون مفهومه معقولاً بالقياس إلى الغير هو النسبة وأقسامه سبعة الأول الأين وهو حصول الجسم في المكان أي في الحيز الذي يخصه ويكون مملوءاً به هذا أيناً حقيقياً به ویسمی وعرفوه أيضاً بأنه هيئة تحصل للجسم بالتسعة إلى مكانه الحقيقي ( وقد يقال ) الأين ا لكونه) وحصوله (في) ما ليس حقيقياً من أمكنته ( مثل الدار أو البلد ) أو الإقليم أو المعمورة أو غير المعمورة أو غير ذلك (مجازاً) أي قولاً مجازياً فإن كل واحد منها يقع في جواب أين هو
قوله : ( وعرفوه أيضاً إلخ) أي قالوا : إنَّ الأين هي الهيئة المترتبة على الحصول في الحيز لكن في ثبوت أمر وراء الحصول تردد .
(And the first) which is that whose concept is understood in relation to another is relation (al-nisba), and its divisions are seven. The first is al-ayn (where), which is the body's obtaining in place, i.e., in the space that belongs to it and is filled by it. This is a real 'where' and is called such. They also define it as a quality that obtains for the body due to its relation to its real place. (And it may be said) that 'where' is the being and obtaining of the body in what is not a real place among its places, such as the house or town, or region, or inhabited area, or uninhabited area, or other than that, metaphorically, i.e., a metaphorical expression, for each of these can be given in answer to the question 'where is it?' His statement: (And they also defined it, etc.) means they said: al-ayn is the quality consequent upon obtaining in space, but there is hesitation about the existence of something beyond the obtaining.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage defines the category of 'where' (al-ayn) as the first of the seven types of relation (al-nisba). The real definition is the body's presence in its proper place, filling that space. A second definition is given: a quality that arises from the body's relation to its real place. The passage then explains that the term 'where' can be used metaphorically for broader locations (house, town, region) because these can answer the question 'where?'. The commentary notes that the second definition (as a quality) is debated: some see it as merely the obtaining itself, while others posit an additional attribute.

Key Terms
الأين (al-ayn)
النسبة (al-nisba)
الحصول (al-ḥuṣūl)
المكان (al-makān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any place answerable to the question 'where' is a real 'where'.
Correction: The real 'where' specifically requires the body's obtaining in its own proper space that it fills.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the real 'where' (al-ayn al-haqiqi) is defined as:
A) The body's presence in any place
B) The body's obtaining in its own space
C) A quality arising from relation to time
D) The answer to the question 'where'

Key Takeaway
The real 'where' (al-ayn) is the body's occupation of its own proper space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you answer 'where are you?' in your spiritual life, do you give a relative answer (e.g., 'trying' or 'struggling') or a real answer that reflects your actual state before Allah? Today, identify one area where you have been satisfied with a relative 'where' and commit to occupying that space with your whole being, just as a body fills its own proper space.
?
The real 'where' requires the body to be in its own proper space. Is your heart in its proper space—submission to your Lord—or is it scattered across worldly concerns? What is one distraction you will remove today so that your heart can truly settle in its real place?
?
This lesson teaches that al-ayn is not just any location but the body's own space. In your daily life, do you treat your body as a trust from Allah, occupying only what is lawful, or do you let it dwell in forbidden spaces? What is one place (physical or metaphorical) you must leave to return to your proper space?

Metaphorical 'Where' and the Second Definition Debate
Sanusi explains metaphorical 'ayn and the second definition debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'where' (al-ayn) is used metaphorically for broader locations and the scholarly hesitation regarding its second definition as a quality.

Core Idea
Al-ayn can be metaphorical when applied to house or town.

Having understood the real meaning of 'where', we now turn to its metaphorical applications. Sanusi explains that terms like 'house' or 'town' are used metaphorically when answering 'where'. The passage also discusses a second definition of 'ayn as a quality, which some scholars hesitate to accept.

Guided Path
1
Read the metaphorical usage
Notice that broader locations like house, town, region are given as examples of metaphorical 'where'. The passage says each can answer 'where is it?'.
These are not the body's own space, so they are metaphorical.
2
Understand the second definition
The commentary expands on the definition of 'ayn as a quality. Consider the hesitation: is it merely obtaining or an additional attribute?
3
Reflect on the distinction
Why does the author include both real and metaphorical? How does this affect kalam discussions about attributes?
This connects to broader theological debates.

Refuting the View that Ayn is an Additional Quality
Refutes the claim that 'where' is a distinct quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the claim that 'where' (al-ayn) is an additional quality beyond the body's occupation of place.

Core Idea
Al-ayn is not an extra attribute—it is simply the relation of occupying a place.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful logical argument against the claim that 'where' is a separate quality. The argument uses a dilemma: either this alleged quality is relational or not. Each horn leads to a problem, ultimately showing that the idea of an additional quality is untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'إما ... وإما' (either ... or) which sets up the dilemma.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The argument divides the possible nature of the alleged quality into two: relational or non-relational. Find where this division occurs and trace each branch.
The non-relational branch leads to quantity/quality; the relational branch reduces to occupation.
3
Understand the conclusion and the burden of proof
The conclusion is that no additional quality is needed. The opponent must first explain what this quality could be and then prove its existence.
The final sentence highlights the burden of proof: 'whoever claims something else must convey its conception and then establish proof.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وعرفوه أيضاً بأنه هيئة تحصل للجسم إلخ ) قال الإمام في المباحث المشرقية : زعم بعضهم أن الأين ليس عبارة عن حصول الجسم في مكانه بل عن هيئة تتم بالنسبة إلى المكان وهذا ضعيف لأنَّ تلك الهيئة إما أن تكون أمراً نسبياً وإما أن لا تكون فإن لم تكن أمراً نسبياً وقد بينا في حصر عدد المقولات أنَّ الأعراض التي لا تكون نسبية فهي إما كميات أو كيفيات فيلزم أن يكون الأين إما كمياً أو كيفياً وهو باطل وأما إن كان أمراً نسبياً فتلك النسبة ليست إلى شيء آخر بل النسبة إلى المكان بالحصول فيه وذلك هو المطلوب وأيضاً النسبة إلى المكان بالحصول فيه أمر معلوم فمن ادعى أمراً آخر فلا بد أن يفيد تصوره ثم يقيم الحجة على ثبوته .
His statement: (And they also defined it as a quality that obtains for the body, etc.) The Imām said in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya: Some claim that al-ayn is not the body's obtaining in its place but rather a quality that is realized in relation to the place. This is weak, because that quality is either a relational matter or not. If it is not relational, and we have already shown in the enumeration of the categories that accidents which are not relational are either quantities or qualities, then it follows that al-ayn is either a quantity or a quality, which is false. But if it is relational, then that relation is to nothing other than the place by obtaining in it, and that is what is sought. Moreover, the relation to the place by obtaining in it is a known matter, so whoever claims something else must convey its conception and then establish proof for its existence.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage contains a logical refutation of the view that 'where' (al-ayn) is an additional quality over and above the body's spatial occupation. The argument is attributed to the Imām (likely al-Rāzī) and proceeds as a dilemma: the alleged quality is either relational or non-relational. If non-relational, it must be either quantity or quality (since only these two categories are non-relational according to the established classification), but 'where' is clearly neither, so that is false. If relational, then its relation is to the place by way of occupation, which is already the very concept of occupation itself — thus no extra quality is needed. The argument concludes that the opponent must first explain what this extra quality could be and then provide proof for its existence, which they cannot.

Key Terms
الأين (al-ayn)
الهيئة (al-hayʾa)
النسبة (al-nisba)
المقولات (al-maqūlāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'where' (al-ayn) is an additional quality beyond the body's occupation of place.
Correction: The passage shows that 'where' is merely the relation of occupation; it is not an extra attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, if the alleged quality of 'where' is non-relational, what must it be?
A) A relation or a substance
B) A quantity or a quality
C) A time or a place
D) An action or a passion

Key Takeaway
The concept of 'where' does not require an additional quality beyond the body's occupation of place; it is simply that relational fact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you tend to multiply concepts unnecessarily, assuming that every perceived difference implies a separate reality? How can this lesson's logical economy guide you to avoid such errors in your understanding of Allah's attributes?
?
When you encounter a claim that seems plausible, do you instinctively examine its logical structure—like the dilemma here—or do you accept it at face value? What would it cost you to adopt the burden of proof mindset this passage demands?
?
The opponent failed to conceive of the extra quality clearly. Are there beliefs you hold about Allah or existence that you have not carefully conceived? Will you commit to examining them today, even if it means discarding comfortable notions?

Definition of Mata (When) and Its Division into Real and Metaphorical
Defines 'when' and distinguishes real vs metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the category 'when' (mata) in Islamic theology, its definition as obtaining in time or its extremity, and the distinction between real when (like 'today') and metaphorical when (like 'week').

Core Idea
Real time is shared by many; real place is not.

The concept of 'when' (mata) is one of the categories used in kalām to discuss temporal attributes. This passage defines it and clarifies its real and metaphorical applications. It also explains how instantaneous sounds relate to time, and how real time differs from real space.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully to get a sense of its flow. Then read the literal English translation. Note any terms that seem technical.
The passage is from a classical text on kalām.
2
Understand the definition of mata
The passage defines 'mata' as either the obtaining itself in time or the quality consequent upon that obtaining. Also includes time's extremity, the 'now'.
There is a disagreement among scholars about whether it is the obtaining or the quality.
3
Distinguish real vs. metaphorical mata
Real mata is exemplified by 'today' for fasting, meaning the whole day. Metaphorical mata includes week, month, year, which are composed of parts but still answer 'when?'.
The test: can the time period be shared by multiple events?
4
Contrast with 'where' and note the instantaneous letters
Unlike place, real time can be shared by many simultaneous events. The passage also explains that instantaneous sounds (like tāʾ) are like points in time and do not cause a problem when composing words.
This relates to the nature of time as continuous vs. discrete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني متى وهو الحصول ) أو الهيئة التابعة للحصول ( في الزمان أو طرفه ) وهو الآن ( كالحروف الآتية ( الحاصلة دفعة مثل التاء والطاء وينقسم المتى كالأين إلى حقيقي كاليوم للصوم وغير حقيقي كالأسبوع والشهر والسنة لما وقع في بعض أجزائها فإنه يجوز أن يجاب بها للسؤال بمتى إلا أنَّ الزمان في المتى الحقيقي يجوز أن يشترك فيه كثيرون بخلاف المكان في الأين الحقيقي
قوله : ( أو الهيئة التابعة ) على اختلاف بينهم .
قوله : ( الحاصلة دفعة وهي التي لا يمكن تمديدها أصلا فإنها لا توجد إلا في آخر زمان حبس النفس كما في لفظة بيت وفرط وولد أو في أوله كما في لفظة تراب وطرب ودور أو في وسطهما كما إذ وقعت هذه الصوامت في أوساط الكلمات فهي بالنسبة إلى الصوت كالنقطة والآن بالنسبة إلى الخط والزمان كذا ذكر الشارح قدس سره في مباحث الحرف فلا إشكال في تركب الألفاظ مع أنها زمانية عن الحروف الآنية على ماوهم .
قوله : ( يجوز أن يشترك فيه كثيرون ) بناء على أنَّ ظرفية الزمان لشيء ليس إلا مقارنته إياه .
The second is mata (when), which is the obtaining or the quality consequent upon obtaining in time or its extremity (which is the now), like the letters that come (occurring instantaneously) such as tāʾ and ṭāʾ. And mata, like ayn, is divided into real, such as 'today' for fasting, and non-real, such as week, month, year, because it occurs in some of their parts, for it is permissible to answer the question 'when?' with them, except that time in real when can be shared by many, unlike place in real where. His statement: (or the consequent quality) indicates disagreement among them. His statement: (occurring instantaneously) are those that cannot be extended at all, for they exist only at the end of the time of breath retention, as in the word 'bayt', 'faraṭ', 'walad', or at its beginning as in 'turāb', 'ṭarab', 'dūr', or in the middle when these plosives occur in the middles of words. They are to sound like a point and the 'now' to the line and time. Thus, the commentator (quddisa sirruh) mentioned in the discussions of the letter that there is no problem in the composition of words, although temporal, from instantaneous letters, contrary to what is imagined. His statement: (it can be shared by many) is based on the fact that the temporality of time for something is nothing other than its being simultaneous with it.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage defines the category 'when' (mata) as either the obtaining in time or the quality that follows from that obtaining. It includes time's boundary (the now). Examples of instantaneous entities are given: letters like tāʾ and ṭāʾ that occur at a single moment. Like 'where', 'when' is divided into real (e.g., 'today' for fasting) and metaphorical (week, month, year) because these are inclusive of parts but still answer 'when?'. A key difference: real time can be shared by many simultaneous events, whereas real place is exclusive. The commentary discusses the disagreement over whether 'when' is the obtaining itself or an additional quality. Further, it explains that instantaneous letters are like points in time, and their combination into words does not cause a problem since they are successive, not actually simultaneous.

الزمان
الآن
الحروف
حقيقي
مجازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mata refers only to a single, discrete moment in time.
Correction: Mata includes both real (whole time periods like 'today') and metaphorical (composite periods like 'week') uses.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes real 'when' (mata) from real 'where' (ayn) according to the text?
A) Real when is exclusive, real where is shared
B) Real when can be shared by many, real where is exclusive
C) Real when is divisible, real where is indivisible
D) Real when is metaphorical, real where is real

Key Takeaway
In kalām, 'when' (mata) is a temporal category with real and metaphorical senses; real time is shareable by multiple entities, unlike real place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your time is more valuable than others' time, forgetting that real time is shared equally by everyone? How does this truth about time challenge your arrogance or impatience?
?
This passage says real time can be shared by many. When you consider your daily schedule, do you treat your time as exclusive property, or do you acknowledge that your time is shared with Allah and others? What would change if you truly believed that?
?
The 'now' is instantaneous. Are you living in the 'now' for Allah, or are you distracted by the metaphorical extensions of week, month, year? How can you anchor yourself in the present moment of obedience?

Defining the Category of Position (Wadʿ)
Defines the category of position as a bodily relation between parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the category of position (wadʿ) is defined as a bodily quality arising from the arrangement of parts.

Core Idea
Position is the internal arrangement of a body's parts, not its location in space.

In this lesson, we examine the third category of the ten Aristotelian categories in Islamic thought: al-wadʿ (position). The text defines it precisely as a quality that arises in a body due to the relation of its parts to one another, distinguishing it from other categories like 'where'. Let's read the passage and commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes a parenthetical clarification about the parts being actual or potential.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms 'الوضع' (position), 'الهيئة' (quality), 'النسبة' (relation), and 'الأجزاء' (parts). Understand how each term contributes to the definition.
3
Distinguish actual vs. potential parts
The commentary explains that the relation of parts includes both actual parts (like separate limbs) and potential parts (like homogeneous parts of a continuous body). Reflect on why this distinction matters for the definition.
This shows the category applies to both structured and homogeneous bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث الوضع وهو هيئة تعرض للشيء ) أي للجسم ) بسبب نسبة أجزائه بعضها إلى بعض
قوله : ( بسبب نسبة أجزائه ( سواء كانت الأجزاء بالفعل أو بالقوة.
The third is al-waḍʿ (position), which is a quality that occurs to a thing (i.e., to a body) due to the relation of its parts to one another. His statement: (due to the relation of its parts), whether the parts are actual or potential.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage defines the category of position (al-waḍʿ) as a quality that arises in a body from the arrangement of its parts relative to each other, regardless of whether those parts are actually separated or only potentially so. The commentary clarifies that the parts may be actual (e.g., separate limbs) or potential (e.g., homogeneous parts of a continuous body). This definition distinguishes position from other relational categories like 'where' (which relates the body to external space) or 'when' (time).

Key Terms
الوضع (al-waḍʿ)
الهيئة (al-hayʾa)
النسبة (al-nisba)
الأجزاء (al-ajzāʾ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Position is the same as the category of 'where'.
Correction: Position concerns the internal arrangement of parts, while 'where' is about the body's location in space.

Checkpoint
What causes the quality of position (wadʿ) to occur in a body?
A) The body's relation to its place
B) The body's existence in time
C) The relation of the body's parts to one another
D) The body's relation to other bodies

Key Takeaway
Position is defined as a quality arising from the internal arrangement of a body's parts, whether actual or potential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sit in tashahhud or prostrate, do you consciously attend to the internal arrangement of your limbs—their relation to one another—or is your position merely habitual? How would your prayer change if you treated each position as a deliberate quality arising from the relation of your parts?
?
Your body's position is not just where you are, but how your parts relate internally. Have you ever considered that your spiritual state might be mirrored by the way you physically compose yourself? What would it mean to pray with your position fully intentional, as a quality of your heart and limbs together?
?
The lesson says position applies even to continuous things with only potential parts. If your own spiritual life feels 'continuous' or unarticulated, does it still have a 'position' before Allah? How might you bring actual distinction to your actions, making your worship a consciously arranged relation of parts—intention, movement, stillness?

Definition of 'Ayn (Where) and Its Divisions
Al-Jurjani defines 'ayn and divides it, then al-Razi refutes an alternative view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the category of 'ayn (where) is defined in kalam, its division into real and metaphorical, and the philosophical dispute about its true nature.

Core Idea
'Ayn is not a quality but a relational category indicating a body's location.

In this passage, al-Jurjani begins by defining the category of 'ayn (where) and explains its two senses: real and metaphorical. He then reports a controversy about whether 'ayn is an additional state or merely the relation of being in place. Imam al-Razi offers a powerful argument that the relation itself is sufficient, and any claim of an extra attribute is either contradictory or unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'ayn
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note that the first part defines 'ayn as the body's being in its specific place (real 'ayn) and then extends to metaphorical usage.
Pay attention to the Arabic terms الحقيقي and المجازي.
2
Understand the metaphorical usage
The second part explains that 'ayn can be used metaphorically for places like a house or country. Think about why these are not real places for the body.
Metaphorical usage answers 'where is it?' but does not give the precise spatial location.
3
Examine the alternative definition
The gloss mentions that some define 'ayn as a state (هيئة) consequent upon being in place, and there is doubt about an extra reality beyond the state.
This sets up the philosophical dispute.
4
Follow al-Razi's argument
Imam al-Razi critiques the view that 'ayn is a non-relational state. He uses a disjunctive argument: if the state is not relative, it falls under quantity or quality, which is false; if relative, it is just the relation of being in place, which is already accepted.
This argument shows that 'ayn is inherently relational, not a separate attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المكان أي في الحيز الذي يخصه ويكون مملوءاً به هذا أيناً حقيقياً به ویسمی وعرفوه أيضاً بأنه هيئة تحصل للجسم بالتسعة[؟] إلى مكانه الحقيقي ( وقد يقال ) الأين لكونه وحصوله (في) ما ليس حقيقياً من أمكنته ( مثل الدار أو البلد ) أو الإقليم أو المعمورة أو غير المعمورة أو غير ذلك (مجازاً) أي قولاً مجازياً فإن كل واحد منها يقع في جواب أين هو . قوله : ( وعرفوه أيضاً إلخ) أي قالوا : إنَّ الأين هي الهيئة المترتبة على الحصول في الحيز لكن في ثبوت أمر وراء الحصول تردد . قوله : ( أو الهيئة التابعة ) على اختلاف بينهم . قوله : ( وعرفوه أيضاً بأنه هيئة تحصل للجسم إلخ ) قال الإمام في المباحث المشرقية : زعم بعضهم أن الأين ليس عبارة عن حصول الجسم في مكانه بل عن هيئة تتم بالنسبة إلى المكان وهذا ضعيف لأنَّ تلك الهيئة إما أن تكون أمراً نسبياً وإما أن لا تكون فإن لم تكن أمراً نسبياً وقد بينا في حصر عدد المقولات أنَّ الأعراض التي لا تكون نسبية فهي إما كميات أو كيفيات فيلزم أن يكون الأين إما كمياً أو كيفياً وهو باطل وأما إن كان أمراً نسبياً فتلك النسبة ليست إلى شيء آخر بل النسبة إلى المكان بالحصول فيه وذلك هو المطلوب وأيضاً النسبة إلى المكان بالحصول فيه أمر معلوم فمن ادعى أمراً آخر فلا بد أن يفيد تصوره ثم يقيم الحجة على ثبوته .
In the place, i.e. in the specific space which it occupies and which is filled by it, this is a real 'ayn (where). And they also defined it as a state that occurs for the body by relation to its true place. And it is said: 'ayn is its being and its occurrence in what is not its true place, such as the house or the country or the region or the inhabited world or the uninhabited world or other than that, metaphorically, i.e. a metaphorical expression, for each of these answers the question 'where is it?' His saying: (And they also defined it, etc.) i.e. they said: 'ayn is the state consequent upon being in the place, but there is hesitation regarding the existence of a thing beyond the state. His saying: (Or the subsidiary state) according to their difference. His saying: (And they also defined it as a state that occurs for the body, etc.) Imam [al-Razi] said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: Some claimed that 'ayn is not an expression for the body's being in its place, but rather for a state that is completed relative to the place. This is weak, because that state is either relative or not. If it is not relative, and we have shown in the enumeration of the categories that non-relative accidents are either quantities or qualities, then 'ayn would have to be either quantitative or qualitative, which is false. And if it is relative, then that relation is to nothing else but the relation to the place by being in it, and that is what is sought. Also, the relation to the place by being in it is a known matter. So whoever claims another thing must first convey its conception and then establish proof for its existence.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of the category 'ayn (where) and then engages in a theological dispute about its nature. First, it defines 'ayn as the body being in its specific place (haqiqi) or in a metaphorical place (majazi), such as house or country. Then a gloss explains that some define 'ayn as the state resulting from being in place, but there is doubt about an additional reality beyond the relation. The main part is a refutation by Imam al-Razi of the view that 'ayn is a distinct quality, not merely relational. Al-Razi argues: if the alleged state is not relative, it must be a quantity or quality, which is false; if it is relative, it is simply the relation of being in place, which is already known. Thus the alternative view is unnecessary.

Key Terms
الأين
الحيز
الحقيقي
المجازي
المقولات
الإمام الرازي
المباحث المشرقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'ayn is a distinct quality or attribute separate from the relation of being in place.
Correction: Imam al-Razi argues that if 'ayn were non-relative it would have to be a quantity or quality, which is false; if relative, it reduces to the relation of being in place, which is the accepted meaning. Thus, 'ayn is purely relational.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, if 'ayn were a non-relative attribute, what would it necessarily fall under?
A) Quantity or quality
B) Relation
C) Substance
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
'Ayn is fundamentally a relational concept, not an independent quality, and any claim of an extra state is either contradictory or redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you tend to think of yourself as occupying a space (a physical location) or as having a state of being? How does the lesson's insistence that 'ayn is purely relational challenge your default perception of your own 'whereness'? Will you reflect today on how your sense of place relates to your awareness of God's omnipresence?
?
The lesson refutes a claim that adds an unnecessary abstraction. In your daily worship, are there habits or beliefs you treat as 'extra states' — rituals devoid of relational meaning? Commit to stripping one such practice down to its core relational reality with your Creator.
?
Al-Razi demands that anyone who claims an extra reality must first convey its conception and then prove it. Is there any article of faith you accept without clear conception from revelation or sound reason? Definition of Mata and Its Divisions
Definition of mata and division into real and metaphorical with excursus on instantaneous letters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mata (when) as a theological term and its division into real (haqiqi) and metaphorical (majazi), including the reasoning behind shared time.

Core Idea
Time in kalam is not a container; it is defined by concurrence and divided into real and metaphorical.

Al-Jurjani now turns to the term 'mata' (when), which deals with time. Just as he defined the term for place ('ayn'), he provides a precise definition for time, distinguishing between real time (like a specific day) and metaphorical time (like a week or month). This passage clarifies how time can be shared and how even instantaneous letters challenge our understanding of temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of mata
Read the Arabic passage once to get its overall meaning. Then read the translation carefully. Pay attention to the phrase 'الحصول أو الهيئة التابعة للحصول في الزمان أو طرفه' which defines mata as occurrence or the state subsidiary to occurrence in time or its extremity (the now).
2
Understand the division into haqiqi and majazi
The passage states that mata, like 'ayn (where), is divided into real and metaphorical. Real mata is a specific time like 'the day for fasting', while metaphorical is an interval like 'the week' or 'month' because the event occurs in some part of it.
3
Examine the instantaneous letters example
Al-Jurjani gives an example of letters that occur at once (like taa and t'aa). These cannot be extended and exist only in the last moment of breath, its beginning, or middle. Compare them to a point on a line or the now in time.
This example illustrates the concept of 'the now' (الآن).
4
Reflect on shared time
The passage ends by noting that time in real mata can be shared by many events because temporality is simply concurrence (مقارنة). In contrast, real place cannot be shared.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني متى وهو الحصول أو الهيئة التابعة للحصول في الزمان أو طرفه وهو الآن كالحروف الآتية الحاصلة دفعة مثل التاء والطاء وينقسم المتى كالأين إلى حقيقي كاليوم للصوم وغير حقيقي كالأسبوع والشهر والسنة لما وقع في بعض أجزائها فإنه يجوز أن يجاب بها للسؤال بمتى إلا أنَّ الزمان في المتى الحقيقي يجوز أن يشترك فيه كثيرون بخلاف المكان في الأين الحقيقي . قوله : ( الحاصلة دفعة وهي التي لا يمكن تمديدها أصلا فإنها لا توجد إلا في آخر زمان حبس النفس كما في لفظة بيت وفرط وولد أو في أوله كما في لفظة تراب وطرب ودور أو في وسطهما كما إذ وقعت هذه الصوامت في أوساط الكلمات فهي بالنسبة إلى الصوت كالنقطة والآن بالنسبة إلى الخط والزمان كذا ذكر الشارح قدس سره في مباحث الحرف فلا إشكال في تركب الألفاظ مع أنها زمانية عن الحروف الآنية على ماوهم . قوله : ( يجوز أن يشترك فيه كثيرون ) بناء على أنَّ ظرفية الزمان لشيء ليس إلا مقارنته إياه .
Second: mata (when) is the occurrence or the state subsidiary to occurrence in time or its extremity, which is the now, like the instantaneous letters occurring at once such as ta' and ta'. And mata, like 'ayn, is divided into real, like the day for fasting, and metaphorical, like the week, month, and year, because they occur in some of their parts, for it is permissible to answer the question 'when?' with them. Except that time in the real mata can be shared by many, unlike place in the real 'ayn. His saying: (occurring at once, which cannot be extended at all, for they exist only in the last moment of breath retention as in the word 'bayt' and 'farat' and 'walad', or in its beginning as in the word 'turab' and 'tarab' and 'dur', or in its middle when these consonants occur in the middle of words. They are to sound like the point and the now to the line and time. Thus mentioned the commentator (may God sanctify his spirit) in the discussions of the letter. So there is no difficulty in the composition of words, even though they are temporal, from instantaneous letters, contrary to what is imagined. His saying: (can be shared by many) is based on the fact that the temporality of something is only its concurrence with it.)
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage defines the category mata (when). It explains that mata is the occurrence of something in time or at the instant (now). It gives the example of instantaneous consonants (like the Arabic letters ta' and t') which occur at once. Then it divides mata into real, like a specific day, and metaphorical, like a week or month, because the time interval includes parts. A key point is that real time can be shared by multiple events, unlike real place. The gloss then expands on the example of instantaneous letters, explaining that they cannot be extended; they occur at the beginning, end, or middle of the breath. This is compared to points on a line. The commentator's discussion resolves the possible objection that words composed of such letters are temporal. The second gloss explains that shared time is based on the notion of being temporally concurrent.

Key Terms
المتى
الزمان
الآن
الحقيقي
المجازي
الحروف الآنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is a simple container that can hold only one event at a time.
Correction: The passage explains that time is defined by concurrence; multiple events can share the same real time because temporality is just being concurrent with it.

Checkpoint

B) Because the event occurs in some part of them

D) Because they are shared

Key Takeaway
Mata (when) is defined as occurrence in time, divided into real (specific time) and metaphorical (interval containing the event), with the key property that real time can be shared by multiple events through concurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson says time is not a container but mere concurrence. When you look at your day, are you aware that each moment is filled with multiple concurrences? How can this awareness change the way you prioritize your actions for the sake of Allah?
?
The instantaneous letters show that even a single breath holds significance. Is there a moment today that you will intentionally fill with dhikr, knowing that your 'now' can be shared with the Divine presence?
?
Real time can be shared by many events, but your attention is limited. What one action will you choose to focus on right now to ensure that your moment is truly lived for the hereafter, not just passed through?

Definition of Position and Its Two Essential Relations
Al-Jurjani defines wad' and argues for its dependence on two relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theologians defined position (wad') as a state caused by two relations—internal part-relations and external relations—and why internal relations alone are insufficient.

Core Idea
Position is not just how parts relate to each other, but how they relate to the outside world.

Al-Jurjani here dissects the concept of wad' (position) to show that it is more than mere internal arrangement of a body's parts. He argues that external relations to directions and surroundings are just as essential. This distinction becomes critical when distinguishing similar positions like standing and being upside down. Let's follow his argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the argument unfold in the author's own words before analyzing.
The passage is dense; read it slowly.
2
Identify the two relations
Notice the two relations mentioned: internal (parts to each other) and external (parts to external things like directions). Understand how they together define position.
The word 'نسبة' (relation) is key.
3
Consider the counterexample
The author presents a thought experiment: if only internal relations mattered, standing and being upside down would be the same. Verify this by imagining a rigid body turned over.
This shows the necessity of external relations.
4
Examine the objection and refutation
The objection says they could share a genus and differ by differentia. Al-Jurjani refutes this by arguing genus and differentia are existentially united—they cannot be separated.
This is a subtle point about metaphysical composition.
5
Understand the conclusion and al-Razi's definition
The consensus is that wad' is a simple state caused by both relations, not a composite. Al-Razi's definition is shown to be compatible, as his restriction implies external relations.
Both definitions point to the same reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث الوضع وهو هيئة تعرض للشيء ) أي للجسم ) بسبب نسبة أجزائه بعضها إلى بعض ) بالقرب والبعد والمحاذاة وغيرها ( و ) بسبب نسبة أجزائه ) إلى الأمور الخارجة ) عن ذلك الشيء كوقوع بعضها نحو السماء مثلا وبعضها نحو الأرض وإذا جعل الوضع هيئة معلولة لنسبتين معا ( فالقيام والاستلقاء وضعان ( متغايران ) لاختلاف نسبة الأجزاء ) فيهما ( إلى الخارج ولو لم يعتبر في ماهية الوضع نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأمور الخارجة بل اكتفى فيها بالنسبة فيما بين الأجزاء وحدها لزم أن يكون القيام بعينه الانتكاس لأن القائم إذا قلب بحيث لا تتغير النسبة فيما بين أجزائه كانت الهيئة المعلولة لهذه النسبة وحدها باقية بشخصها فيكون وضع الانتكاس وضع القيام بعينه لا يقال اللازم مما ذكرتم اشتراكهما في معنى الوضع الذي هو جنسهما فجاز أن يفترقا بالفصل الحاصل من النسبة الخارجية لأنَّا نقول الجنس والفصل يتحدان وجوداً وجعلاً فكيف يتصوران حصة من الجنس قارنت فصلاً ثم فارقته إلى فصل آخر فالحق إذن اعتبار النسبتين في ماهية الوضع . قوله : ( وإذ جعل الوضع إلخ) اتفقوا على أن الوضع هيئة بسيطة معلولة للنسبتين وليس مركباً منهما إذ النسبة فيما بين الأجزاء أو فيما بينها وبين الأمور الخارجة ليس إلا القرب والبعد والمحاذاة والمجاورة والتماس وليس القيام والقعود نفس تلك النسب ولا مركباً من الهيئتين الحاصلتين من تينك النسبتين إذ لا دليل على وجودها في القيام مثلا فضلا عن تركبه منهما فهو هيئة وحدانية معلولة لهما فتدبر فإنه مما زل فيه الأقدام واعلم أنه عرف الإمام الوضع في المباحث المشرقية : بأنه هيئة تحصل للجسم بسبب نسبة بعض أجزائه إلى بعض نسبة تخالف الأجزاء لأجلها بالقياس إلى الجهات كالموازاة والانحراف ولا تخالف بين التعريفين وإن كان ظاهر هذا التعريف مشعراً بأنه معلول لنسبة الأجزاء فيما بينها لأنه قيد فيه النسبة بكونها موجبة لتخالفها بالقياس إلى الجهات وذلك لا يحصل إلا بعد اعتبار القسمة إلى الأمور الخارجية أيضاً إلا أنَّه في التعريف المشهور جعل معلولاً لمجموع النسبتين وفيما ذكره الإمام معلولاً للنسبة المقيدة .
Third: wad' (position) is a state that occurs to a thing (i.e. to a body) due to the relation of its parts to each other (by proximity, distance, alignment, etc.) and due to the relation of its parts to external things, like some parts being towards the sky and some towards the earth. And if wad' is made a state caused by both relations together, then standing and lying down are two different wad's due to the difference in the relation of the parts to the external. And if no account were taken in the essence of wad' of the relation of the parts to external things, but only the relation among the parts themselves were considered, then standing would be exactly the same as being upside down, because if the standing person is turned over so that the relation among his parts does not change, the state caused by this relation alone would remain individually the same, so the wad' of being upside down would be exactly the wad' of standing. It cannot be said: the necessitation from what you mentioned is their sharing in the meaning of wad', which is their genus, so they may differ by the differentia resulting from the external relation. Because we say: the genus and differentia are united in existence and constitution. How then can a portion of the genus be conceived as having a differentia and then leaving it for another differentia? So the truth is to take account of both relations in the essence of wad'. His saying: (And if wad' is made) etc. They agreed that wad' is a simple state caused by the two relations, not composed of them. For the relation among the parts or between them and external things is only proximity, distance, alignment, adjacency, and contact. And standing and sitting are not the same as those relations, nor are they composed of the two states resulting from those two relations, since there is no proof for their existence in standing, for example, let alone composition from them. Rather, it is a unitary state caused by them. So contemplate, for it is a place where feet slip. And know that Imam [al-Razi] defined wad' in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya as: a state that occurs for the body due to the relation of some of its parts to each other, a relation that makes the parts differ with respect to directions, like parallelism and deviation. There is no conflict between the two definitions, even though the apparent sense of this definition suggests that it is caused by the relation among the parts, because he restricts the relation to that which causes their difference with respect to directions, which cannot happen without also taking account of the division to external things. However, in the famous definition it is made caused by the sum of the two relations, and in what Imam mentioned it is made caused by the restricted relation.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage defines the category wad' (position) and rigorously argues that it depends on two types of relations: the relation of a body's parts to each other (internal) and their relation to external things (like directions). The definition is illustrated with examples: standing and lying down are different positions because the parts have different relations to the external (e.g., head towards sky vs. towards earth). A hypothetical objection is considered: if only internal relations mattered, then standing and being upside down would be the same position because the internal arrangement of parts is the same. The objection that they differ by a differentia is refuted: genus and differentia are existentially united, so they cannot be separated. Thus both relations are essential. The commentary then notes that authorities agree wad' is a simple state caused by the two relations, not a composite. Imam al-Razi's definition, which mentions only internal relation but with a condition that it causes difference in external directions, is compatible with the famous definition.

الإمام الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Position only depends on how a body's parts are arranged relative to each other.
Correction: Position also depends on how those parts relate to external things like directions; otherwise standing and being upside down would be indistinguishable.

Checkpoint
If position were defined only by internal part-relations, what would be the problematic consequence?
A) Standing and sitting would be the same
B) Standing and being upside down would be the same
C) Lying down would be undefined
D) External relations would be redundant

Key Takeaway
Position (wad') is a simple state caused by both internal part-relations and external relations to the surroundings, and both are necessary to distinguish different positions like standing and being upside down.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you reduce a person’s spiritual position (their faith or sincerity) to only their internal state, ignoring the external pressures and circumstances that shape their actions? Recall a specific judgment you made that would have benefited from considering both internal and external relations. What will you do differently now?
?
The passage warns that ignoring external relations collapses standing and being upside down. In your life, what is a role or commitment (e.g., prayer, marriage, work) that you have treated as merely internal, only to later realize that your orientation toward Allah—the external relation—is what truly defines it? How will you reassess that today?
?
Al-Jurjani refutes the idea that the external relation is just a differentia added to a common genus. Consider your worship: Do you treat the external form (like posture in prayer) as a mere addition to a generic 'submission', or as an essential part of the unique act it is? How can you embody that unity between internal attitude and external action starting now?
