Objection Regarding Relation to Quality
Second objection: quality relation is not limited to action and passion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the relation to quality is not limited to action and passion but can also be by similarity.

Core Idea
Quality relation is not confined to acting and being acted upon.

The second objection challenges the claim that the relation to quality (kayf) is necessarily captured by the categories of action (fi‘l) and passion (infi‘āl). The opponent argues that there could be a relation by similarity (mushābaha) and that the confinement of these two categories to quality relation is not proven.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the second objection. Focus on the two main points: (1) relation by similarity is possible, (2) the confinement of action/passion to quality is open to scrutiny.
The commentator glosses 'these two' as action and passion.
2
Understand the alternative relation
The opponent introduces 'mushābaha' (similarity) as a possible relation. Think of examples: a red object and a red light share a relation of similarity in color, which is a relation to quality, but neither is acting on the other.
3
Question the confinement to action/passion
The objection also questions why action and passion must be the only ways to relate to quality. Perhaps other kinds of relations exist. This undermines the necessity of the categories.
The passage says 'the confinement of these two categories to relation to quality is open to scrutiny.'
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider how this objection weakens the claim that nine categories are exhaustive. If quality relation can be other than action/passion, then either the categories are incomplete or their definitions need revision.
This is a logical objection, not a final refutation.

?

?

Objection Regarding Relation to Substance
Third objection: relation to substance can be inherence or locus, and concluding challenge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the relation to substance can be conceived as inherence or a locus, challenging the necessity of the ninefold division.

Core Idea
Substance relations include inherence and being a locus, not just one.

The third objection targets the relation to substance (jawhar). The opponent argues that accidents can relate to substance in two ways: by inhering in it (ḥulūl) or by being a locus (ḥāyiz) for it. These are distinct relations, so the classification might be incomplete or misdefined. The passage concludes that the absence of these divisions is not necessary, and the burden of proof is on the defender of the ninefold division.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the third objection. Notice the two relations: inherence (ḥulūl) and being a locus (ḥāyiz).
The commentator expands: 'I mean the inherence of accidents in the substance itself, and being a locus for it.'
2
Distinguish the two relations
Understand the difference: one is the accident's relation to its substrate (inherence), the other is the substrate's relation to the accident (being a locus). These are reciprocal but distinct.
3
Recognize the concluding challenge
The final sentence asserts that the nonexistence of these divisions is not necessary (i.e., not self-evident), and the opponent demands proof from the defender.
This flips the burden of proof.
4
Synthesize all three objections
Consider how all three objections together undermine the claim of necessity. The ninefold division may be based on induction or convenience, not logical necessity.
The lesson ends with a key takeaway about the nature of such classifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed a classification of ideas was complete without considering if there might be overlooked distinctions? How can this lesson's challenge to the ninefold division prompt you to examine your own assumptions in matters of faith?
?
When you hear a theological argument that seems to 'cover all possibilities,' do you accept it uncritically, or do you seek proof? What would it mean for your spiritual life to adopt the humility of requiring proof for claims of exhaustiveness?
?
Consider a relationship in your life (e.g., with family or with Allah) that you tend to see as a simple, one-directional connection. How might the insight that the same relata can have distinct relations (inherence vs. locus) help you deepen that relationship?

Definition of Accident Transfer
Defines accident transfer and states need for proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of accident transfer in kalam and why a proof is needed to deny its possibility.

Core Idea
Accident transfer is conceivable; denial requires a proof.

Before we can discuss whether accidents can move between substrates, we must define what we mean by 'transfer' for both substances and accidents. This lesson establishes that definition and sets up the need for argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author defines transfer for substances vs. accidents.
The passage begins by stating the objective: that the accident does not transfer.
2
Compare transfer of substance and accident
The author clarifies that the common definition of transfer (as change of locus) applies to substances. For accidents, it means subsisting in a new substrate after another.
3
Understand the need for proof
The author asserts that accident transfer is not inconceivable, so denying it requires a proof. This sets up the later objection and answer.
This is a key methodological point: claims about impossibility need demonstration.
4
Reflect on the definition
Think about why accidents might be thought incapable of transfer. The definition helps isolate the specific issue.
Accidents are not spatially extended, so moving them requires a different notion of transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : أن العرض لا ينتقل من محل إلى محل هو كان في حيز آخر وهذا المعنى لا يتحقق إلا في المتحيز والعرض ليس بمتحيز ( وفيه نظر فإن ذلك ( الانتقال المفسر بما ذكر ) هو انتقال الجوهر ) من مكان إلى آخر ( وأما انتقال العرض الذي كلامنا فيه ) فهو أن يقوم عرض بعينه بمحل بعد قيامه بمحل آخر ) وليس هذا مما لا يتصور في العرض بل لا بد لنفيه عنه من برهان ( لا يقال حال الانتقال أما في المحل الأول أو الثاني وكلاهما باطل لأن كونه في المحل الأول استقرار فيه متقدم على الانتقال عنه وكونه في المحل الثاني ثبوت فيه متأخر عن الانتقال إليه وأما في محل آخر فيعود الكلام إلى انتقاله إلى هذا المحل ويلزم ذلك المحذور ( لأنا نقول جاز أن يكون انتقال العرض دفعياً لا تدريجياً فيكون آن مفارقته عن آن مقارنته لمحل آخر )
The First Observatory - The Fifth Objective: That the accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. It is that it was in another locus, and this meaning is only realized in what is spatially extended, and an accident is not spatially extended. (And there is consideration: indeed that (transfer explained as mentioned) is the transfer of the substance) from one place to another. (But as for the transfer of the accident which we are speaking about) it is that a specific accident subsists in a substrate after having subsisted in another substrate. And this is not something inconceivable for the accident; rather, a proof is necessary for its negation. (It cannot be said: during transfer, it is either in the first or second substrate, and both are invalid. For its being in the first substrate is a stability therein prior to transfer away from it, and its being in the second substrate is a fixing therein subsequent to transfer to it. And as for being in another substrate, the discussion returns to its transfer to this substrate, and that problematic consequence follows.) (Because we say: it is possible that the transfer of the accident be instantaneous, not gradual, so that the moment of its separation is the moment of its conjunction with another substrate.)
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines what is meant by 'transfer' (intiqal) for accidents and then presents and refutes an objection against its possibility. The passage begins by stating the objective: that an accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. It then defines transfer for a substance as going from one locus (hiz) to another, but for an accident it means a specific accident subsisting in a second substrate after having subsisted in a first. The author notes that this is not inconceivable; a proof is needed to deny it. The objection states that during transfer, the accident is either in the first substrate (which would mean it is still there, contradicting transfer), or in the second (which would mean it has already arrived, also contradicting transition), or in some third substrate, which leads to infinite regress. The answer is that the transfer can be instantaneous (daf'iyyan) rather than gradual. In an instantaneous transfer, the moment of separation from the first substrate is exactly the moment of conjunction with the second, avoiding the need for an intermediate state. This is a common argument in kalam regarding motion and change, where motion is seen as a series of discrete instants rather than a continuous process.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
انتقال (transfer)
محل (substrate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of accident transfer?
A) An accident moving from one spatial location to another
B) An accident subsisting in a substrate after having subsisted in another substrate
C) An accident ceasing to exist in one substrate and being created anew in another
D) An accident changing its substance

Key Takeaway
Accident transfer means a specific accident subsisting in a new substrate after subsisting in a previous one, and it is not inherently impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that accident transfer is conceivable and that denying it requires proof. Think of a belief you hold that you have never really examined—what would it take for you to require proof for that belief? Are you willing to test it?
?
The author distinguishes between two types of transfer: one for substances and one for accidents. In your own life, do you mistakenly treat spiritual or moral attributes (accidents of the soul) as if they were physical substances? How does that confusion affect your self-criticism?
?
The passage says 'there is consideration' before correcting a definition. When was the last time you reconsidered a definition you took for granted? What would it mean for you to question a comfortable assumption today—and act on the answer?

Refuting the Instantaneous Objection
Presents and refutes objection against accident transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against accident transfer and its refutation through the concept of instantaneous transfer.

Core Idea
Instantaneous transfer avoids the dilemma of being in two places at once.

The objection claims that during transfer the accident must be either in the first or second substrate, both problematic. The answer introduces the concept of instantaneous (daf'i) transfer, where the moment of separation and arrival are the same. This lesson examines that argument and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection presents a dilemma: during transfer, the accident is either in the first substrate (contradicting departure), in the second (contradicting arrival), or in a third (leading to regress). Understand each horn.
The first two options are considered invalid because they imply the accident is 'stable' in one place, not in transition.
2
Identify the three options
The objection lists three possibilities: being in the first, second, or another third substrate. The first two are dismissed; the third leads to infinite regress.
This is a classic logical dilemma (trilemma).
3
Understand the answer: instantaneous transfer
The answer posits that transfer can be instantaneous (daf'iyyan), not gradual. In that case, the moment of separation from the first substrate is exactly the moment of conjunction with the second, so no intermediate state exists.
This avoids the need for the accident to be in any substrate during a 'transition period'.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this answer fits within the kalam atomistic view of time, where moments are discrete. Instantaneous transfer becomes plausible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(And there is consideration: indeed that (transfer explained as mentioned) is the transfer of the substance) from one place to another. (But as for the transfer of the accident which we are speaking about) it is that a specific accident subsists in a substrate after having subsisted in another substrate. And this is not something inconceivable for the accident; rather, a proof is necessary for its negation. (It cannot be said: during transfer, it is either in the first or second substrate, and both are invalid. For its being in the first substrate is a stability therein prior to transfer away from it, and its being in the second substrate is a fixing therein subsequent to transfer to it. And as for being in another substrate, the discussion returns to its transfer to this substrate, and that problematic consequence follows.) The Argument Against Accident Transfer
Jarjani states the main argument against accident transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that accidents cannot transfer from one substrate to another because their individuation is dependent on their substrate.

Core Idea
Accidents are individuated by their substrate; loss of substrate destroys identity, preventing transfer.

Jarjani presents a concise but powerful objection to the idea that accidents can move between substrates. The argument hinges on the nature of individuation: if an accident's particularity is tied to its location, then leaving that location would erase its identity. Consequently, what appears elsewhere must be a new accident, not a transferred one. This passage is a key case of applying the principle that every particularization needs a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the key concepts
Notice the terms: 'tashakhkhus' (individuation), 'huwiyya' (identity), 'mahall' (substrate). Understand how they interrelate.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow the argument from the premise that substrate influences individuation to the conclusion that transfer is impossible. Note the intermediate steps about identity persistence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : أن العرض لا ينتقل من محل إلى محل غيره ترجيح بلا مرجح (فهو أي تشخصه لمحله) فالحاصل في المحل الثاني هوية أخرى أي تشخص آخر غير التشخص الذي كان حاصلا في المحل الأول لأنه لما كان لمحله مدخل في تشخصه لم يتصور مفارقته عنه باقياً تشخصه بل يجب انتفاؤه حينئذ فلا يكون الحاصل في المحل الآخر عين الذي عدم بل شخصاً آخر من نوعه والانتقال من محل إلى آخر لا يتصور إلا مع بقاء الهوية المنتقلة من أحدها إلى الآخر وإذ لا بقاء للهوية هاهنا فلا انتقال أصلاً
The First Observatory - The Fifth Intention: That an accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. Other than it is a preference without a preponderant. (So it means its individuation [is due to] its substrate). So what obtains in the second substrate is another identity, i.e., another individuation other than the individuation that was obtaining in the first substrate. Because since its substrate has an influence on its individuation, its separation from it while its individuation remains is inconceivable. Rather, its negation must [occur] then. So what obtains in the other substrate is not the very thing that ceased to exist, but another individual of its type. And transfer from one substrate to another is inconceivable except with the persistence of the identity that transfers from one to the other. And since there is no persistence of identity here, there is no transfer at all.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents the main argument against the transfer of accidents. It begins by stating the position: an accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. The proof is that the accident's individuation (tashakhkhus) is tied to its substrate (mahall). Therefore, if it left its substrate, its individuation would cease, and what appeared in the second substrate would be a new individual of the same type, not the original accident. Transfer requires persistence of identity (huwiyya), but since the identity is lost upon separation, transfer is impossible. The argument relies on the principle that particularization requires a cause, and the substrate plays a role in that particularization.

Key Terms
عرض
محل
تشخص
هوية
انتفاء
انتقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'transfer' means spatial movement of an accident, like a color moving from one object to another.
Correction: In kalam, transfer means the accident continues to exist while moving to a different substrate, losing its original substrate. The argument shows this would destroy its identity, not just change location.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the claim that accidents do not transfer?
A) Because accidents are eternal.
B) Because their individuation is tied to their substrate.
C) Because they are not real.
D) Because they cannot exist in two places.

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot transfer because their identity is inseparably linked to their substrate; losing the substrate means losing the accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your deeds are accidents tied to your soul in this life. When you die, they cease to exist with your body. You cannot transfer your good deeds to another or store them for later. How does this truth change how you approach every action today, knowing it will be judged uniquely in this moment?
?
If accidents cannot transfer, then the identity you have now—your reputation, your knowledge, your attachments—will not follow you after death. What is the one attachment you must sever now because it is not based on what endures?

The Objection and the Three Meanings of Identity
Jarjani enumerates three meanings of identity and their logical consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Jarjani argues that the accident's individuation cannot be through its identity or quiddity, and what the three possible meanings of identity entail.

Core Idea
Identity (huwiyya) is ambiguous; if it means individuation itself, self-causation follows.

Jarjani objects to the claim that the accident's individuation is through the substrate. He begins by noting that individuation could be due to something specific, not the substrate. To show this, he examines three possible meanings of 'identity' (huwiyya) and demonstrates that each leads to an absurdity. This passage lays the groundwork for the deeper analysis of causality in accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise style and the logical structure.
2
Identify the three meanings of identity
The passage presents three possibilities for what 'identity' might mean when connected to individuation. List them.
They are: specific individuation, quiddity plus individuation, specific existence.
3
Understand the consequences
For each meaning, Jarjani shows a logical absurdity. Reflect on why each is problematic.
Self-causation, whole causing part, and the third leads to further issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون تشخصه الخاصة ولا يلزم حينئذ انحصار النوع في الشخص إنما يلزم ذلك إذا كان بهويته تشخصه بماهيته وفيه بحث لأنه إن أريد بهويته الخاصة تشخصه لزم كون الشيء علة لنفسه وإن أريد ماهيته مع تشخصه كان الكل علة لجزئه وإن أريد وجوده العيني فإن قوله: (لمحله) إما بنفسه أو بما حل فيه فيكون للمحل مدخل فيه فلا يرد أن هاهنا احتمالاً آخر وهو أن يكون تشخصه بما حل في محله كذا قيل وفيه أنه حينئذ يجوز الانتقال عليه لأن المحل لا دخل له في العلية إلا باعتبار الحلول لما هو علة لتشخص العرض فيه وفي شرح المقاصد في رد الاحتمال المذكور أنا ننقل الكلام إلى تشخص ذلك الأمر ويرجع الأمر إلى المحل دفعاً للدور والتسلسل وأورد عليه أنه يجوز أن يحل في محل العرض على سبيل التعاقب أمور غير متناهية يكون كل سابق معداً للتشخص ومثله جائز عند الحكماء والجواب أنَّ الكلام في العلة الفاعلية للتشخص فيجب اجتماعها.
And there is a consideration: It is possible that its individuation is [due to] something specific, and then the confinement of the species to the individual does not necessarily follow. That only follows if its individuation were by its identity through its quiddity. And there is discussion: because if what is meant by its specific identity is its individuation, then a thing being a cause of itself would follow. And if what is meant is its quiddity together with its individuation, then the whole would be a cause of its part. And if what is meant is its specific existence, then... He said: (its substrate) either by itself or by what inheres in it, so the substrate has influence in it. So it is not refuted that there is another possibility: that its individuation is by what inheres in its substrate. Thus it was said. And in it: that then transfer is possible because the substrate has no role in causality except by virtue of the inherence of what is the cause of the accident's individuation in it. And in Sharh al-Maqasid, in refuting the mentioned possibility, [it is said]: We transfer the discussion to the individuation of that thing and the matter returns to the substrate to prevent circularity and infinite regress. And it was objected that it is possible that non-finite things inhere in the substrate of the accident in succession, each prior being a preparation for the individuation, and the like is permissible according to the philosophers. And the answer: the discussion concerns the efficient cause of individuation, so it must coexist.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: perhaps the accident's individuation is due to something specific other than the substrate, such as a property, so it could transfer. The response examines the three possible meanings of 'identity' (huwiyya): (1) if identity means the specific individuation itself, then it would be self-causation; (2) if it means quiddity together with individuation, the whole would cause its part; (3) if it means specific existence, it leads to further problems. Then it considers the possibility that individuation comes from something inhering in the substrate. Two objections are raised: (a) if the substrate only mediates inherence, the transfer becomes possible; (b) there could be an infinite succession of inhered causes. The answer is that the efficient cause must coexist, not be a series of preparers.

Key Terms
تشخص
هوية
ماهية
وجود عيني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'identity' (huwiyya) has a single unambiguous meaning in this context.
Correction: Jarjani demonstrates that identity can refer to three different things: the individuation itself, the quiddity plus individuation, or the specific existence.

Checkpoint
What follows if 'specific identity' is taken to mean the individuation itself?
A) Infinite regress.
B) The thing being a cause of itself.
C) Circularity.
D) The whole being cause of its part.

Key Takeaway
The term 'identity' in kalam must be carefully distinguished; otherwise, absurd consequences like self-causation arise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even technical terms like 'identity' can be ambiguous and lead to absurd conclusions if misused, how carefully are you defining the key concepts in your own understanding of Allah and His attributes? Could a vague or sloppy definition be leading you to an incorrect belief without your realizing it?
?
The passage shows that failing to distinguish between meanings can lead to the impossible consequence of self-causation. In your own theological reasoning, are there places where you might be conflating different meanings of a term (e.g., 'will', 'knowledge', 'power') and thus arriving at an understanding that inadvertently separates you from the truth? What step will you take today to become more precise in your thinking about such concepts?

Inherence-Based Individuation and the Efficient Cause
Jarjani examines inherence-based individuation and defends the necessity of simultaneous efficient cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that if the accident's individuation is caused by something inhering in the substrate, the substrate's role is derivative, and the efficient cause must coexist.

Core Idea
The efficient cause of individuation must coexist; infinite succession of preparers is insufficient.

After dismissing the three meanings of identity, Jarjani considers a new possibility: the accident's individuation could be due to something inhering in the substrate. He argues that this would allow the accident to transfer, because the substrate only contributes by providing a locus for the inherence. Then, he refutes an objection about infinite regress by appealing to the nature of the efficient cause, which must coexist with its effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the new possibility
The text introduces the idea that individuation might come from something inhering in the substrate. Read the Arabic and translation.
2
Analyze the objection about transfer
Why does this possibility make the accident potentially transferable? Because the substrate only mediates inherence, not causality.
Jarjani says 'the substrate has no role in causality except by virtue of the inherence.'
3
Understand the response from Sharh al-Maqasid
The response shifts the discussion to the individuation of that inhering thing, eventually leading back to the substrate to avoid circularity and infinite regress.
This is a typical kalam strategy.
4
Grasp the philosophers' objection and the answer
Philosophers might allow an infinite succession of inhering causes. The answer is that the efficient cause must be simultaneously present, not a series of preparers.
This is a key point about causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون تشخصه الخاصة ولا يلزم حينئذ انحصار النوع في الشخص إنما يلزم ذلك إذا كان بهويته تشخصه بماهيته وفيه بحث لأنه إن أريد بهويته الخاصة تشخصه لزم كون الشيء علة لنفسه وإن أريد ماهيته مع تشخصه كان الكل علة لجزئه وإن أريد وجوده العيني فإن قوله: (لمحله) إما بنفسه أو بما حل فيه فيكون للمحل مدخل فيه فلا يرد أن هاهنا احتمالاً آخر وهو أن يكون تشخصه بما حل في محله كذا قيل وفيه أنه حينئذ يجوز الانتقال عليه لأن المحل لا دخل له في العلية إلا باعتبار الحلول لما هو علة لتشخص العرض فيه وفي شرح المقاصد في رد الاحتمال المذكور أنا ننقل الكلام إلى تشخص ذلك الأمر ويرجع الأمر إلى المحل دفعاً للدور والتسلسل وأورد عليه أنه يجوز أن يحل في محل العرض على سبيل التعاقب أمور غير متناهية يكون كل سابق معداً للتشخص ومثله جائز عند الحكماء والجواب أنَّ الكلام في العلة الفاعلية للتشخص فيجب اجتماعها.
And there is a consideration: It is possible that its individuation is [due to] something specific, and then the confinement of the species to the individual does not necessarily follow. That only follows if its individuation were by its identity through its quiddity. And there is discussion: because if what is meant by its specific identity is its individuation, then a thing being a cause of itself would follow. And if what is meant is its quiddity together with its individuation, then the whole would be a cause of its part. And if what is meant is its specific existence, then... And the answer: the discussion concerns the efficient cause of individuation, so it must coexist.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: perhaps the accident's individuation is due to something specific other than the substrate, such as a property, so it could transfer. The response examines the three possible meanings of 'identity' (huwiyya): (1) if identity means the specific individuation itself, then it would be self-causation; (2) if it means quiddity together with individuation, the whole would cause its part; (3) if it means specific existence, it leads to further problems. Then it considers the possibility that individuation comes from something inhering in the substrate. Two objections are raised: (a) if the substrate only mediates inherence, the transfer becomes possible; (b) there could be an infinite succession of inhered causes. The answer is that the efficient cause must coexist, not be a series of preparers.

Correction: The passage argues that the efficient cause of individuation must coexist with its effect, so a chain of successive preparers is insufficient.

Key Takeaway
The efficient cause of individuation must be simultaneous with its effect; an infinite regress of preparatory causes is not sufficient.

The Substrate's Role: Absolute or Specific?
Jarjani answers an objection about the substrate's role being absolute or specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection regarding whether the substrate's influence on the accident's individuation is absolute or specific, and the response that refutes both possibilities.

Core Idea
A substrate cannot individuate an accident unless it is specific, but specificity leads to alternating causes.

In this passage, Jarjani addresses a subtle objection about the substrate's role in individuating an accident. The objection suggests that perhaps the substrate's influence is absolute, not tied to any particular substrate. Jarjani's response dismantles this by showing that neither an absolute substrate nor a specific one can work—each leads to an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the objection is introduced by 'قيل' and the response by 'وفيه'.
The objection tries to allow the accident to leave its substrate.
2
Analyze the response's first part
Consider why an absolute substrate cannot cause individuation. The commentary explains that an absolute quiddity lacks specificity, so it cannot produce a specific effect.
3
Analyze the response's second part
If the substrate is taken as specifically any given substrate, then multiple specific substrates would serve as alternative causes for the same individuation, which is impossible because a single effect cannot have multiple causes in succession.
This is the 'alternation of causes' (توارد العلل) argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لأنه لما كان لمحله مدخل إلخ ) قيل : يجوز أن يكون مدخلية المحل في تشخصه من حيث أنه محل ما لا محل معين ما لا محل معين فيجوز مفارقته وفيه أنَّ المحل المطلق كيف يوجب تشخص العرض وإن أريد به المحل المعين أي معين كان يلزم توارد العلل على سبيل البدل على معلول واحد شخصي أعني تشخص العرض.
His statement: (because since its substrate has influence etc.) It is said: It is possible that the influence of the substrate in its individuation is in that it is a substrate absolutely, not a specific substrate, so it can leave it. And in it: How does the absolute substrate cause the individuation of the accident? And if what is meant is the specific substrate, whichever specific one, then the occurrence of causes in alternation upon a single individual effect (namely the accident's individuation) would follow.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents a specific objection and response. The objection is that perhaps the substrate's influence on the accident's individuation is merely that it is a substrate in general, not a particular substrate, so the accident could leave its substrate and still be individuated by any substrate. The response has two parts: (1) an absolute substrate cannot cause a specific individuation—it lacks specificity. (2) If we take the substrate as specifically any given substrate, then multiple specific substrates would act as alternative causes for the same individuation, which is impossible because a single effect cannot have multiple causes in succession (alternation).

Key Takeaway
An absolute substrate cannot cause a specific effect, and a specific substrate leads to alternating causes, which is impossible.

Alternative Proof on Accident Transfer
Jarjani presents an alternative proof and answers multiple objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an alternative kalam proof for the impossibility of accident transfer and the key objections and responses that refine the understanding of causality and individuation.

Core Idea
An accident cannot transfer because its individuation ties it to a specific substrate.

This passage presents a different argument for the same conclusion: an accident cannot move from one substrate to another. The argument uses a dichotomy about the kind of substrate the accident needs. Several objections are raised and answered, each one deepening our understanding of how causes and individuation work. You will see how the kalam tradition refines its claims through dialectical engagement.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof's dichotomy
Read the opening proof carefully. It argues that the accident must need either a specific or non-specific substrate, and the non-specific leads to absurdity.
Notice the logical structure: if non-specific, the accident would not exist externally.
2
First objection: substrate without specificity
The first objection suggests the accident may need a substrate without the condition of being specific. This challenges the dichotomy by positing a middle option.
This objection is based on analogy with form and matter.
3
Answer: distinguishing causes
The answer distinguishes the cause of individuation from the cause of persistence. The one-in-person requires a single specific cause, so an absolute substrate cannot individuate.
This uses the principle that the one-in-person has a single cause in person.
4
Remaining objections and refinements
Examine the later objections: alternation of causes, separate cause, and accidents confined to a single individual. Each is answered, showing the robustness of the proof.
These objections test the boundaries of the proof's applicability.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And perhaps it is said in proving the impossibility of transfer: The accident needs the substrate necessarily. So either the specific accident needs a substrate and does not leave it (because the particularity of that specific accident is related to that specific substrate and requires it essentially), or it needs a non-specific substrate, and that has no existence in the external world, because everything existing in the external world is specific in itself. Commentary
This passage presents an alternative proof for the impossibility of accident transfer, followed by a series of objections and answers. The proof uses a dichotomy: the accident must need either a specific substrate (then it cannot leave) or a non-specific substrate (which does not exist externally). Therefore, transfer is impossible. The first objection posits that the accident may need a substrate without the condition of specificity, like form needing matter. The answer distinguishes between cause of individuation and cause of persistence: the one-in-person requires a single specific cause, so an absolute substrate cannot be the cause of individuation. Other objections consider the possibility of the substrate being specific but not as this particular one, leading to alternation of causes; the possibility of a separate cause; and the exception of accidents whose species is confined to one individual. Each is refuted.

Objection from Sense Transfer of Accidents and Its Answer
Objection and two-part answer: transfer of accidents defended by sensory evidence, answered by creation or emanation of a similar accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that sensory experience of transfer (e.g., apple smell) seems to contradict the impossibility of accident transfer, and the two main answers (theologian and philosopher) that explain it as creation or emanation of a new similar accident.

Core Idea
Sensory transfer is not real transfer but creation or emanation of a new accident.

The claim that accidents cannot transfer from one locus to another seems contradicted by everyday experience: we perceive the smell of an apple moving to an adjacent object, or heat from fire transferring to something that touches it. This objection forces theologians and philosophers to clarify what actually happens. The following passage presents the objection and two classical answers—one from the theologians (mutakallimun) and one from the philosophers (falasifa).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from sense
Identify the sensory examples given (smell, heat) and understand why they appear to prove transfer of accidents.
Note that the objector is using common experience to challenge a rational principle.
2
Analyze the first answer (theologians)
The theologians (us) say a new similar accident is created by the free agent (God) by way of habit upon adjacency or contact. Recognize that this preserves the impossibility of transfer by positing a fresh creation.
The term 'habit' (ʿādah) is key: God's usual practice, not a necessary law.
3
Analyze the second answer (philosophers)
The philosophers say the new accident emanates from the Active Intellect due to a disposition acquired from contact. Notice that both answers agree on the core point: the same accident does not move.
philosophers invoke necessary emanation, contrasting with the theologians' voluntary creation.
4
Reflect on the common misconception
The passage guards against the mistaken belief that what we sense is literal transfer. Understand why this is important for the broader kalam discussion on accidents and causality.
This connects to the larger debate about causality and God's role in natural events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل هذا الذي ذكرتموه من امتناع الانتقال على العرض (إنكار للحس فإنَّ رائحة التفاح تنتقل منه إلى ما يجاوره والحرارة تنتقل من النار إلى ما يماسها كما يشهد به الحس ) فالجواب أن الحاصل في المحل الثاني ( وهو المجاور أو المماس ) شخص آخر من الرائحة أو الحرارة مماثل للأول الحاصل في التفاح أو النار ) يحدثه الفاعل المختار عندنا بطريق العادة عقيب المجاورة أو المماسة ( أو يفيض ) ذلك الشخص الآخر على المحل الثاني ( من العقل الفعال ) عند الحكماء بطريق الوجوب ) لاستعداد يحصل له من المجاورة ) أو المماسة
For it is said: This that you have mentioned of the impossibility of transfer regarding the accident (is a denial of sense-perception, for the smell of the apple transfers from it to what is adjacent to it, and heat transfers from fire to what contacts it, as sense-perception testifies). The answer: That which occurs in the second locus (which is the adjacent or contacting thing) is another individual of the smell or heat, similar to the first individual occurring in the apple or fire, (which is produced by the free agent according to us, by way of habit, after adjacency or contact) (or that other individual emanates) upon the second locus (from the Active Intellect) according to the philosophers, by way of necessity, (due to a disposition that occurs to it from adjacency) or contact.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector raises a sensory counterexample: we perceive accidents like the smell of an apple transferring to an adjacent object, or heat from fire transferring to something that touches it. If the author claims that an accident cannot move from one locus to another, then this sensory evidence contradicts that. The answer explains that what is perceived is not the same accident moving but a new, similar accident arising in the second locus. According to the theologians (us), the free agent (God) creates this new accident by habit (ʿādah) upon adjacency or contact. According to the philosophers, it emanates from the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl) due to a disposition (istiʿdād) acquired by the second locus from the contact. Thus, there is no actual transfer of the same accident; rather, a new accident is generated or emanated, preserving the impossibility of an accident subsisting in another accident or moving as an individual.

Key Terms
العرض
الانتقال
الفاعل المختار
العادة
العقل الفعال
الاستعداد
المجاورة
المماسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sensory experience of smell spreading or iron heating proves that the same accident physically moves from one object to another.
Correction: What actually occurs is a new, similar accident created by God (theologians) or emanating from the Active Intellect (philosophers) in the second locus; the original accident does not transfer.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in the text, when we perceive the smell of an apple transferring to an adjacent object, what is actually happening?
A) The same smell accident moves from the apple to the adjacent object.
B) A new, similar smell accident is created or emanated in the adjacent object.
C) The smell accident multiplies itself into two loci.
D) The apple loses its smell entirely.

Key Takeaway
The perceived transfer of accidents is not a real movement of the same accident but the creation or emanation of a new, similar accident in the second locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Two Proofs for Impossibility and Their Refutation
Presents two proofs against accident-in-accident subsistence and labels them weak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs used by kalam scholars to argue that an accident cannot subsist in another accident, and why these proofs are considered weak.

Core Idea
Accidents are attached to substances, not to each other.

The question of whether one accident can inhere in another is a foundational issue in kalam cosmology. Most rational theologians deny it, but the philosophers affirm it. In this passage, two key proofs are presented to support the kalam position, though the commentator later shows they rest on a questionable definition of subsistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the two proofs are labeled as 'the first' and 'the second aspect'.
The passage ends by stating both proofs are weak.
2
Analyze the first proof
The first proof argues that subsistence (qiyām) means secondary spatialization (taḥayyuz tabaʿī). This requires the host to be spatially extended by itself (mutaḥayyiz bi-dhāt). Since accidents are not spatially extended by themselves, they cannot host another accident.
This proof relies on a specific definition of subsistence.
3
Analyze the second proof
The second proof uses an infinite regress argument: a constitutive accident cannot subsist by itself; if it subsists in another accident, that accident would need a constitutive accident, leading to an infinite regress. The regress must end at a substance, so all accidents ultimately subsist in the substance, not in another accident.
This proof assumes that the chain cannot continue infinitely.

لا يجوز قيام العرض بالعرض عند أكثر العقلاء خلافاً للفلاسفة. لنا) في عدم الجواز وجوه والمذكور في الكتاب وجهان (الأول) أن قيام الصفة) بالموصوف ( معناه تحيز الصفة تبعاً لتحيز الموصوف وهذا أي كون الشيء متبوعاً لتحيز غيره به ( لا يتصور إلا في المتحيز بالذات لأن المتحيز بتبعية غيره لا يكون متبوعاً لثالث إذ ليس كونه متبوعاً لذلك الثالث أولى من كونه تابعاً له ) والعرض ليس بمتحيز ) بالذات ، بل هو تابع في التحيز للجوهر ( فلا يقوم به غيره. الوجه الثاني : العرض المقوم به ) لا يجوز أن يقوم بنفسه و ( إن قام بعرض آخر عاد الكلام فيه وتسلسل الأعراض المقوم بها إلى غير النهاية ( وإلا فجميع تلك الأعراض المتسلسلة حاصلة (لا) في محل وقد عرفت بطلانه لامتناع قيام العرض واحداً كان أو متعدداً بنفسه بل لا بد له محل يقوم به ( وإن انتهت ( الأعراض المقوم بها ) إلى الجوهر فالكل قائم به ). وهما ) أي هذان الوجهان ضعيفان أما الأول فلأنا لا نسلم أن القيام هو التحيز تبعاً لما ذكرتم ( بل هو الاختصاص الناعت وهو أن يختص شيء بآخر اختصاصاً يصير به ذلك
It is not permissible for an accident to subsist in an accident according to most rational people, contrary to the philosophers. For us, regarding the impermissibility, there are aspects, and the ones mentioned in the book are two. (The first): That the subsistence of an attribute in the described means the spatialization (taḥayyuz) of the attribute secondarily (tabaʿan) to the spatialization of the described, and this—i.e., a thing being followed in its spatialization by another—(is only conceivable in something spatially extended by itself (mutaḥayyiz bi-dhāt), because something spatially extended secondarily to another cannot be followed by a third, since its being followed by that third is not more appropriate than its being a follower of it). And an accident is not spatially extended by itself; rather, it is a follower in spatialization to the substance, (so no other thing subsists in it). The second aspect: The accident that is constitutive (al-muqawwam) of it (i.e., of another accident) cannot subsist by itself, and (if it subsists in another accident, the discussion recurs and there is an infinite regress of constitutive accidents ad infinitum. (Otherwise, all those serial accidents would be subsisting without a locus, and you have already known its invalidity due to the impossibility of an accident subsisting by itself, whether one or multiple; rather, it must have a locus in which it subsists). And if (the constitutive accidents) end (at the substance, then all of them subsist in it). (And they)—i.e., these two aspects—are weak. As for the first, because we do not grant that subsistence is secondary spatialization as you mentioned; (rather, it is particularizing description (al-ikhtiṣāṣ al-nāʿit), which is that a thing is particularized by another in a particularization by which
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a combination of two kalam proofs and their refutation. It begins by stating the thesis: it is impermissible for an accident to subsist in another accident, a position held by most rational people (al-ʿuqalāʾ) contrary to the philosophers. Two proofs are provided from the book. Proof 1 argues that 'subsistence' (qiyām) means secondary spatialization (taḥayyuz tabaʿī): the attribute becomes spatially extended by being a follower of the substance's spatialization. This secondary spatialization can only be conceived in something that is spatially extended by itself (mutaḥayyiz bi-dhāt), because something that is only secondarily spatialized cannot serve as a source of spatialization for a third thing—it would be equally valid for it to be a follower. Since an accident is not spatially extended by itself (it is a follower of the substance), no other accident can subsist in it. Proof 2 argues by infinite regress: a constitutive accident (i.e., an accident that determines another accident) cannot subsist by itself; if it subsists in another accident, that accident itself would need a constitutive accident, leading to an infinite regress. If the regress ends at a substance, then all the accidents in the chain actually subsist in that substance, not in another accident. Therefore, no accident subsists in an accident. The commentator then declares both proofs weak (ḍaʿīfān). The refutation of the first proof is given: we do not grant that 'subsistence' is secondary spatialization. Instead, it is 'particularizing description' (al-ikhtiṣāṣ al-nāʿit)—a special relation by which one thing characterizes another. This redefinition undermines both proofs because they rely on the spatialization interpretation. The refutation of the second proof is implied by the same redefinition, as the commentary elsewhere indicates.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that these proofs are logically decisive and that the kalam position is irrefutable.
Correction: However, the author immediately declares both proofs weak, indicating that they are not considered conclusive by all. The refutation (not included in this passage) shows that the definition of subsistence as spatialization can be disputed.

Key Takeaway
Two arguments—one based on spatialization and one on infinite regress—are offered against accident-in-accident subsistence, but they are deemed weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author presents two proofs that seem logically sound, yet he labels them weak. When you hold a belief based on reasoning that feels airtight, how do you react if a scholar shows it is flawed? Does your ego resist, or do you genuinely humbly reconsider? Will you commit today to testing your own cherished arguments against the scrutiny of those with deeper knowledge, even when those arguments seem obvious?
?
Both proofs rely on definitions—of subsistence as spatialization, and of constitutive accidents. In your own life, how often do you base important decisions or beliefs on definitions you have never questioned? What would it take for you to trace your own foundational assumptions back to their source and examine them critically, as this lesson does with these proofs?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
5 min
The Doctrine and Its Weak Arguments
States the doctrine and presents two weak arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam ruling that an accident cannot inhere in another accident and the two weak arguments (based on spatial extension and infinite regress) that attempt to justify it.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot inhere in accidents, but the common arguments for this are shaky.

In this passage, the author states the key doctrine that an accident cannot inhere in another accident. He then presents two arguments often used to support it: one based on spatial extension and another on infinite regress. However, he immediately notes that both arguments are weak, setting the stage for the correct understanding of inherence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author presents the doctrine and then two arguments in quick succession.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find the first argument (spatial extension) and the second argument (infinite regress/termination). Notice how each is formulated.
3
Why are they weak?
The author calls both arguments weak. Think about what assumption they share that might be flawed. This will be clarified in the next lesson.
Hint: both rely on a specific definition of 'inherence'.

The First Observatory - Sixth Objective: It is not permissible for an accident to inhere in an accident. ( ( It is only conceivable in what is spatially extended by essence, because what is spatially extended by following something else cannot be followed by a third, since its being followed by that third is not more likely than its being a follower of it. ) And the accident is not spatially extended ) by essence, but rather it is a follower in spatial extension of the substance ( so nothing else inheres in it. The second aspect: The accident that constitutes it ) cannot inhere in itself, and ( if it inheres in another accident, the discussion recurs and the constituting accidents would regress infinitely ( Otherwise, all those regressing accidents would exist without a locus, and you already know its invalidity due to the impossibility of an accident—whether one or multiple—inhering by itself; rather, it must have a locus in which it inheres ( and if the constituting accidents ) terminate ( at the substance, then all ) adhere to it ) because all are followers of that substance in its spatial extension. And thus, no accident inheres in an accident, and the contrary is assumed to be otherwise. ( Both ) i.e., these two aspects are weak. As for the first, we do not grant that inherence is spatial extension, following what you mentioned, ( but rather it is attributive specification, which is that something is specified by another in a specification by which it becomes... ) from one of them due to what is spatially extended by essence and is attributed to what is spatially extended by following, considering that it has a certain relation to what is spatially extended by essence, like the description of the state of the related thing—not that there is one personal spatial extension that inheres in both, and because there are two other spatial extensions—so understand, for feet have slipped in it. His saying: (And the accident is not spatially extended by essence—a second premise for the proof. Its explanation: The followed in spatial extension is spatially extended by essence, and the accident is not spatially extended, so the followed in spatial extension is not an accident. So what is said that this premise is superfluous is an illusion. His saying: (Existing without a locus) because if it existed in a locus, it would not be regressing infinitely. His saying: (And if it terminates) is a conjunction to what is understood from his saying 'and regress', as if it were said: if it does not terminate, regress follows, which is invalid; and if it terminates, etc. His saying: (But it is attributive specification) is the basis for the aforementioned denial, as if it were said: why may it not be that inherence is attributive specification? And by 'specification' is meant connection, and the attribution of the adjective to it is metaphorical because it is a cause for it, as is clarified by the expression of the commentary. His saying: (Because all are followers of that substance in its spatial extension) This reasoning indicates that the pivot of the second inference is also that inherence is in the sense of following in spatial extension. And thus, the answer to the first inference by denying that the meaning of inherence is following in spatial extension is also an answer to the second inference. But because the pivot of what was mentioned for the second inference is not explicitly stated in the author's formulation, unlike its pivoting for the first, the mentioned denial was made an answer only for the first. As for the answer to the second inference, it does not depend on denying the pivot but is an answer even if it is granted that the meaning of inherence is following in spatial extension, as is not hidden. His saying: (But it is attributive specification) The Imam said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: If they say, 'What is the reality of that specification?' We say: 'We have no way to know the essence of that specification except by mentioning this necessary concomitant.' This observatory, sixth objective: It is not permissible for an accident to inhere in an accident. The thing is an adjective for the other, and the other is described by it, so the first is called a state (hal), and the second a locus (mahall) for it, like the specification of the body, not like the specification of water in a jug. (And it verifies it) i.e., it verifies that the meaning of inherence is this, not that, through two matters: The first: That spatial extension is an attribute of the substance inhering in it, and spatial extension is not spatially extended (as a follower of its spatial extension), otherwise the thing (i.e., spatial extension) would be conditional upon itself if we say the spatial extension inhering in that substance is one, for spatial extension must first inhere in the substance so that something else may follow it in spatial extension; so if that something else is spatial extension itself, then its inherence in the substance has been conditioned by its inherence in the substance, which is conditioning a thing by itself. (Or there would be regress) if we say the spatial extensions inhering in the substance are multiple, so that the inherence of each spatial extension in it is conditioned by the inherence of another spatial extension before it, and so on ad infinitum. The second matter: The attributes of God Most High inhere in Him, as we will show, without any taint of spatial extension in His essence and attributes. (And as for) the second (aspect), it does not negate that an accident inheres in a second accident, and that second (accident) in a third, arranged until it terminates at the substance. Thus some of them are followers of that substance in its spatial extension directly, and others are followers of the first batch, and it does not follow from that that all are inhering in the substance and following it in its spatial extension directly; rather, there is what follows it in that through mediation. And the statement that the follower cannot be... His saying: (And it verifies it) is a demonstration that the meaning of inherence is attributive specification, so it is a counterargument to the claim that inherence is spatial extension. For the verifiers have affirmed the validity of counterarguments in definitions. Then the necessary consequent from the two matters is to negate that the meaning of inherence is spatial extension. As for its being specification, a premise must be added, which is that there is no third possibility, so if one is invalidated, the other is established. His saying: (Verily, spatial extension is an attribute, etc.) i.e., an accident inhering in the substance, because spatial location (ayn) is among the real existents by agreement of the philosophers and theologians. So what is said that it is a conceptual thing, so its inherence need not be expressed as following in spatial extension, then the answer that there is no difference between the inherence of an accident and a conceptual thing is an illusion. His saying: And thus ad infinitum, so the body would have in one spatial location infinite spatial locations, and common sense belies it and the proof of correspondence refutes it. That there is no difference between the inherence of the attribute of knowledge, for example, in His essence and its inherence in an essence. His saying: (The second matter, etc.) means the contingent, and there is no taint of spatial extension in it essentially, actually, or potentially, so it is not objected that the inherence of an accident means spatial extension, not absolute inherence, so as to be refuted by the inherence of His attributes in His essence. His saying: (For it does not negate, etc.) means that your statement 'so all are inhering in it' - if you mean by it the inherence of all in it directly, then the concomitance is denied, because termination at the substance does not necessitate that. And if you mean by it the inherence of all in it, even mediately, then the concomitance is granted, but the invalidity of the consequent is denied because it is the point of contention. And if the reality of a thing is not known through its constitutive elements, it does not necessitate the denial of that thing, for most things are known only through their necessary concomitants.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a continuous refutation and verification concerning a key kalam doctrine: that an accident cannot inhere in another accident. The discussion is dense and proceeds in multiple steps. First, the text presents a second weak argument (الوجه الثاني) for the impossibility: if an accident (عرض) were to inhere in another accident, there would be an infinite regress of accidents, which is impossible. Alternatively, if the regress ends at a substance (جوهر), then all accidents in the chain would inhere in that substance, contradicting the assumption that an accident is inhering in another accident. The commentator notes that both arguments presented are weak (ضعيفان). The refutation begins by denying that inherence (قيام) is equivalent to spatial extension (تحيز). Instead, inherence is defined as 'attributive specification' (اختصاص ناعت): a relation where one thing becomes an attribute of another, not identical to spatial occupancy. This is illustrated by the difference between an attribute inhering in a body (like whiteness in a body) versus water being contained in a jug—the latter is not attributive specification. The passage then provides two positive arguments (أمران) to verify this correct meaning. The first argument shows that if inherence were spatial extension, spatial extension itself would either be conditioned by itself (circular) or lead to regress. The second argument appeals to God's attributes: they inhere in His essence without any spatial extension, proving that inherence is not spatial occupancy. Finally, the commentator addresses the second weak argument directly, clarifying that termination at a substance does not require all to inhere directly; some may inhere through mediation, which is the very point disputed. The passage concludes that not knowing a thing's reality through its constitutive elements does not justify denying it, as most things are known through their necessary concomitants.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that inherence is equivalent to spatial extension (تحيز).
Correction: The passage hints that this assumption is flawed, as both arguments rely on it and are deemed weak.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first argument against an accident inhering in an accident?
A) Accidents are created ex nihilo
B) Inherence is only possible for what is spatially extended by essence, and accidents are not
C) Accidents are eternal
D) Substances cannot have accidents

Key Takeaway
The kalam doctrine is that an accident cannot inhere in an accident, but the common arguments based on spatial extension and infinite regress are weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the two common arguments for a foundational kalam doctrine are explicitly called weak by the author. Does this unsettle your confidence in the doctrine itself? If so, what will you do to find a stronger basis for your belief?
?
If the arguments for such a basic rule about accidents are weak, how can you trust other arguments you've encountered in theology? What is your personal responsibility in verifying the proofs of your faith?
?
Are you tempted to blindly accept theological conclusions just because they are widely taught? This lesson shows that even classic proofs can be flawed. Will you commit to examining every argument you learn with critical thought, even if it challenges your comfort?

Inherence as Attributive Specification
Refutes the equation of inherence with spatial extension and defines it as attributive specification, with two verifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct meaning of inherence (قيام) as attributive specification (اختصاص ناعت) and the two verifications that confirm it.

Core Idea
Inherence is not spatial extension; it is a relation of attributive specification.

After stating that the common arguments are weak, the author now provides the correct understanding of inherence. He denies that inherence equals spatial extension and defines it as attributive specification. He then gives two verifications: one showing that spatial extension cannot be the basis, and another appealing to God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Focus on the sentence where the author says inherence is 'attributive specification' (الاختصاص الناعت). Understand the contrast with the earlier view.
The definition emphasizes a relation of one thing being an attribute of another, not spatial co-location.
2
First verification
Notice the argument that if inherence were spatial extension, then spatial extension itself would be subject to circularity or regress. This shows the absurdity of equating them.
3
Second verification
Consider the example of God's attributes: they inhere in His essence without any spatial extension. This proves that inherence can occur without spatial extension.
This is a powerful argument from theology.
4
Reflect on the definition
Think about how this definition of inherence as attributive specification resolves the weakness of the earlier arguments. The earlier arguments assumed inherence is spatial extension, which is now proven wrong.

The First Observatory - Sixth Objective: It is not permissible for an accident to inhere in an accident. ( ( It is only conceivable in what is spatially extended by essence, because what is spatially extended by following something else cannot be followed by a third, since its being followed by that third is not more likely than its being a follower of it. ) And the accident is not spatially extended ) by essence, but rather it is a follower in spatial extension of the substance ( so nothing else inheres in it. The second aspect: The accident that constitutes it ) cannot inhere in itself, and ( if it inheres in another accident, the discussion recurs and the constituting accidents would regress infinitely ( Otherwise, all those regressing accidents would exist without a locus, and you already know its invalidity due to the impossibility of an accident—whether one or multiple—inhering by itself; rather, it must have a locus in which it inheres ( and if the constituting accidents ) terminate ( at the substance, then all ) adhere to it ) because all are followers of that substance in its spatial extension. And thus, no accident inheres in an accident, and the contrary is assumed to be otherwise. ( Both ) i.e., these two aspects are weak. As for the first, we do not grant that inherence is spatial extension, following what you mentioned, ( but rather it is attributive specification, which is that something is specified by another in a specification by which it becomes... ) from one of them due to what is spatially extended by essence and is attributed to what is spatially extended by following, considering that it has a certain relation to what is spatially extended by essence, like the description of the state of the related thing—not that there is one personal spatial extension that inheres in both, and because there are two other spatial extensions—so understand, for feet have slipped in it. His saying: (And the accident is not spatially extended by essence—a second premise for the proof. Its explanation: The followed in spatial extension is spatially extended by essence, and the accident is not spatially extended, so the followed in spatial extension is not an accident. So what is said that this premise is superfluous is an illusion. His saying: (Existing without a locus) because if it existed in a locus, it would not be regressing infinitely. His saying: (And if it terminates) is a conjunction to what is understood from his saying 'and regress', as if it were said: if it does not terminate, regress follows, which is invalid; and if it terminates, etc. His saying: (But it is attributive specification) is the basis for the aforementioned denial, as if it were said: why may it not be that inherence is attributive specification? And by 'specification' is meant connection, and the attribution of the adjective to it is metaphorical because it is a cause for it, as is clarified by the expression of the commentary. His saying: (Because all are followers of that substance in its spatial extension) This reasoning indicates that the pivot of the second inference is also that inherence is in the sense of following in spatial extension. And thus, the answer to the first inference by denying that the meaning of inherence is following in spatial extension is also an answer to the second inference. But because the pivot of what was mentioned for the second inference is not explicitly stated in the author's formulation, unlike its pivoting for the first, the mentioned denial was made an answer only for the first. As for the answer to the second inference, it does not depend on denying the pivot but is an answer even if it is granted that the meaning of inherence is following in spatial extension, as is not hidden. His saying: (But it is attributive specification) The Imam said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: If they say, 'What is the reality of that specification?' We say: 'We have no way to know the essence of that specification except by mentioning this necessary concomitant.' This observatory, sixth objective: It is not permissible for an accident to inhere in an accident. The thing is an adjective for the other, and the other is described by it, so the first is called a state (hal), and the second a locus (mahall) for it, like the specification of the body, not like the specification of water in a jug. (And it verifies it) i.e., it verifies that the meaning of inherence is this, not that, through two matters: The first: That spatial extension is an attribute of the substance inhering in it, and spatial extension is not spatially extended (as a follower of its spatial extension), otherwise the thing (i.e., spatial extension) would be conditional upon itself if we say the spatial extension inhering in that substance is one, for spatial extension must first inhere in the substance so that something else may follow it in spatial extension; so if that something else is spatial extension itself, then its inherence in the substance has been conditioned by its inherence in the substance, which is conditioning a thing by itself. (Or there would be regress) if we say the spatial extensions inhering in the substance are multiple, so that the inherence of each spatial extension in it is conditioned by the inherence of another spatial extension before it, and so on ad infinitum. The second matter: The attributes of God Most High inhere in Him, as we will show, without any taint of spatial extension in His essence and attributes. (And as for) the second (aspect), it does not negate that an accident inheres in a second accident, and that second (accident) in a third, arranged until it terminates at the substance. Thus some of them are followers of that substance in its spatial extension directly, and others are followers of the first batch, and it does not follow from that that all are inhering in the substance and following it in its spatial extension directly; rather, there is what follows it in that through mediation. And the statement that the follower cannot be... His saying: (And it verifies it) is a demonstration that the meaning of inherence is attributive specification, so it is a counterargument to the claim that inherence is spatial extension. For the verifiers have affirmed the validity of counterarguments in definitions. Then the necessary consequent from the two matters is to negate that the meaning of inherence is spatial extension. As for its being specification, a premise must be added, which is that there is no third possibility, so if one is invalidated, the other is established. His saying: (Verily, spatial extension is an attribute, etc.) i.e., an accident inhering in the substance, because spatial location (ayn) is among the real existents by agreement of the philosophers and theologians. So what is said that it is a conceptual thing, so its inherence need not be expressed as following in spatial extension, then the answer that there is no difference between the inherence of an accident and a conceptual thing is an illusion. His saying: And thus ad infinitum, so the body would have in one spatial location infinite spatial locations, and common sense belies it and the proof of correspondence refutes it. That there is no difference between the inherence of the attribute of knowledge, for example, in His essence and its inherence in an essence. His saying: (The second matter, etc.) means the contingent, and there is no taint of spatial extension in it essentially, actually, or potentially, so it is not objected that the inherence of an accident means spatial extension, not absolute inherence, so as to be refuted by the inherence of His attributes in His essence. His saying: (For it does not negate, etc.) means that your statement 'so all are inhering in it' - if you mean by it the inherence of all in it directly, then the concomitance is denied, because termination at the substance does not necessitate that. And if you mean by it the inherence of all in it, even mediately, then the concomitance is granted, but the invalidity of the consequent is denied because it is the point of contention. And if the reality of a thing is not known through its constitutive elements, it does not necessitate the denial of that thing, for most things are known only through their necessary concomitants.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a continuous refutation and verification concerning a key kalam doctrine: that an accident cannot inhere in another accident. The discussion is dense and proceeds in multiple steps. First, the text presents a second weak argument (الوجه الثاني) for the impossibility: if an accident (عرض) were to inhere in another accident, there would be an infinite regress of accidents, which is impossible. Alternatively, if the regress ends at a substance (جوهر), then all accidents in the chain would inhere in that substance, contradicting the assumption that an accident is inhering in another accident. The commentator notes that both arguments presented are weak (ضعيفان). The refutation begins by denying that inherence (قيام) is equivalent to spatial extension (تحيز). Instead, inherence is defined as 'attributive specification' (اختصاص ناعت): a relation where one thing becomes an attribute of another, not identical to spatial occupancy. This is illustrated by the difference between an attribute inhering in a body (like whiteness in a body) versus water being contained in a jug—the latter is not attributive specification. The passage then provides two positive arguments (أمران) to verify this correct meaning. The first argument shows that if inherence were spatial extension, spatial extension itself would either be conditioned by itself (circular) or lead to regress. The second argument appeals to God's attributes: they inhere in His essence without any spatial extension, proving that inherence is not spatial occupancy. Finally, the commentator addresses the second weak argument directly, clarifying that termination at a substance does not require all to inhere directly; some may inhere through mediation, which is the very point disputed. The passage concludes that not knowing a thing's reality through its constitutive elements does not justify denying it, as most things are known through their necessary concomitants.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some equate inherence (قيام) with spatial extension (تحيز).
Correction: The passage proves that inherence is attributive specification, not spatial extension, using logical and theological arguments.

Checkpoint
What two arguments does the author use to verify that inherence is not spatial extension?
A) Infinite regress and God's attributes
B) Self-conditioning of spatial extension and God's attributes
C) Self-conditioning of spatial extension and infinite regress
D) God's attributes and the definition of substance

Key Takeaway
Inherence is attributive specification, not spatial extension; this is verified by the impossibility of spatial extension conditioning itself and by God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes—like His knowledge or power—do you unconsciously imagine them as 'occupying' a place in His essence? How does this lesson challenge that mental image, and what will you do to correct it?
?
The proof shows that equating inherence with spatial extension leads to logical absurdity. Have you ever spoken about God's attributes in a way that treated them as physical or spatial? What specific change in your speech or thought will you make starting now?
?
If inherence is purely attributive, then every attribute of God is a real relation without location. Does this realization deepen your reverence? How will you remind yourself of this when you discuss or reflect on Allah's names and attributes?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate

Refuting the Second Argument and Concluding Points
Refutes the second weak argument and gives a concluding epistemological note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to refute the second weak argument (termination at substance) and why not knowing a thing's essence does not justify denying it.

Core Idea
Termination at substance does not require direct inherence; some inherence is mediated, and unknown essences do not negate reality.

The second weak argument claimed that if accidents terminate at a substance, then all inhere directly in that substance. The author refutes this by distinguishing direct and mediated inherence. He also addresses the objection that we cannot know the essence of something without its constitutive elements, reminding that most things are known through their necessary concomitants.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Focus on the argument that termination at substance does not imply direct inherence for all accidents. Understand the distinction between direct and mediated following.
2
Note the concluding point
The author ends with a remark about knowledge: not knowing a thing's essence through its constitutive parts does not mean we should deny it. We often know things through their properties (لوازم).
This is a general epistemological principle.
3
Connect to the bigger picture
Consider how this refutation completes the discussion. The correct meaning of inherence (attributive specification) allows for mediated inherence, and the weak arguments are fully addressed.

بهما ولأن هناك تحيزين الآخر فافهم فإنه زل فيه أقدام . قوله: (والعرض ليس بمتحيز بالذات مقدمة ثانية للدليل تقريره المتبوع في التحيز متحيزاً بالذات والعرض ليس بمتحيز فالمتبوع في التحيز ليس بعرض فما قيل
